Punishment of Ahl al-Fatrah and Those Who Received the Call
Sanusi summarizes the disagreement on punishment for ahl al-fatrah and those who received the call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which theological groups hold that ahl al-fatrah are punished and that all groups punish those who receive the call but fall short.

Core Idea
Only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila punish ahl al-fatrah; all punish those who get the call and fail.

The author begins by distinguishing two categories: those who never received the prophetic call and those who did but deviated. The punishment of the first group is disputed, while the second is agreed upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two categories.
2
Identify the two groups being discussed
The passage describes who says the one never reached by the call is punished, and who says the one who received but changed is punished.
Note the nuance: only 'some' Maturidis and Mu'tazila, not all.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage mentions that for Maturidis and Mu'tazila, knowledge is based on reason, so they hold the unfavoured accountable.
This rational basis will be contrasted with revelation-based views later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل ما يقال: إن الذي لم تَبْلُغه دعوة لا يقول بتعذيبه إلا بعض المَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ والمعتزلة؛ لبناء أمر المعرفة على العقل عند الفريقين، وأما الذي بَلَغَتْهُ الدعوة، فغَيَّرَ أو قَصَّرَ، فكَفَرَ؛ فيقول بتعذيبه مَنْ ذُكِرَ من الفريقين، والنووي ومن معه، فمتى حَصَلَتِ المعرفة والتوحيد لشخص من أهل الفترة؛ نَجَا باتفاق عند من قال بكفاية العقل لقيامه بالواجب، وَعِنْدَ من قال بالشرع واكتفى بدعوة رسول ما لقيامه بالواجب أيضاً إن كان سَمِعَها، وإلا؛ فلعدم وجوبها عليه وَعِنْدَ مَنْ قال بالشرع بشرط أن يكون مُرْسَلاً إليه لعدم وجوبها عليه، سواءٌ سَمِعَ من رسول لم يُرْسَلْ إليه أو لم يسمع، وتبرُّعُه بها لا يضرُّهُ. ومن هنا يَحْصُلُ جَزْمُكَ بأن أبويه ناجيان؛ لما قيل إنهما كانا على الحنيفية دين إبراهيم كما كان على ذلك طائفة من العرب كزَيْدِ بنِ عَمْرو بن نُفَيْلِ ووَرَقَةَ بنِ نَوْفَلَ، وغيرهما في تلك الأيام، فهما - إن شاء تعالى - في أعلى عليين في دار السلام
The summary of what is said: that the one whom the call has not reached, only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila say he is punished, because the matter of knowledge is based on reason for the two groups. As for the one whom the call reached but changed or fell short, he disbelieves; thus the aforementioned groups and al-Nawawi and those with him say he is punished. Whenever knowledge and tawhid occur for a person from the people of interval, he is saved by agreement according to those who say reason suffices for performing the obligation. And according to those who say (it is based on) revelation and suffice with the call of a certain messenger for performing the obligation, also if he heard it; otherwise, due to its not being obligatory on him. And according to those who say (it is based on) revelation conditioned upon being sent to him, due to its not being obligatory on him, whether he heard from a messenger not sent to him or did not hear; and his voluntarily performing it does not harm him. From here you gain certainty that his parents are saved, because it is said that they were upon the Hanifiyya, the religion of Abraham, as a group of Arabs were upon that, like Zayd ibn Amr ibn Nufayl and Waraqa ibn Nawfal, and others in those days. So they—God willing—are in the highest 'Illiyin in the Abode of Peace.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage summarizes the theological positions on the fate of ahl al-fatrah. The key point: only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila punish those whom the call never reached, because they consider reason as a sufficient basis for obligation. Those who received the call but fell into disbelief are punished by all groups, including Nawawi. For a person from ahl al-fatrah who attains knowledge of tawhid, there is agreement on salvation according to three scenarios: (1) if reason suffices, (2) if revelation suffices and he heard a messenger's call (any messenger), (3) if revelation requires being sent to him specifically, but he was not sent to, so it is not obligatory. The passage then decisively applies this to the Prophet's parents, citing that they were on the Hanifiyya (pure monotheism) like Zayd ibn Amr and Waraqa. Thus they are in paradise.

Key Terms
أهل الفترة
الماتريدية
المعتزلة
النووي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Maturidis and Mu'tazila punish ahl al-fatrah.
Correction: The passage specifies 'some' (بعض), indicating not all.

Checkpoint
Which groups say the one whom the call never reached is punished?
A) All Maturidis and all Mu'tazila
B) Only al-Nawawi and his followers
C) Some Maturidis and some Mu'tazila
D) Only the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Punishment for not receiving the call is disputed among theologians, but for those who receive it and fall short, there is consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever received clear guidance from Allah about a matter, yet you changed it or fell short in acting upon it? What does this passage teach you about the seriousness of such failure?
?
You know the truth of tawhid and the message of Islam. Are you living it fully, or are you among those who 'change or fall short'? What specific step will you take today to align your actions with what you know?
?
If you were among the ahl al-fatrah (those who never heard the call), how would you want to be judged? How does this passage affect your understanding of Allah's justice and mercy?

Three Views on Salvation for Ahl al-Fatrah
Sanusi enumerates three theological views on how ahl al-fatrah can be saved if they achieve tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three different theological bases for the salvation of ahl al-fatrah who attain tawhid.

Core Idea
Salvation for ahl al-fatrah hinges on whether reason suffices, or revelation with or without being sent.

After discussing punishment, the author turns to the positive case: if an ahl al-fatrah person attains knowledge and tawhid, they are saved according to all schools. But the justification differs based on whether one holds reason sufficient, or revelation with various conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three views
Read the Arabic from 'فمتى حصلت' to 'تطوعه بها لا يضره' and its translation.
2
Identify the three views
The passage presents three positions: (1) reason suffices, (2) revelation with any messenger if heard, (3) revelation only if sent to.
Each view leads to salvation under different conditions.
3
Understand the logic of each view
The first says reason is sufficient for obligation; the second requires hearing a messenger; the third requires being sent to.
Notice how the third view is stricter regarding the condition of the message.
4
Note the outcome
All three views agree on salvation if ahl al-fatrah attains tawhid, but the path differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل ما يقال: إن الذي لم تَبْلُغه دعوة لا يقول بتعذيبه إلا بعض المَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ والمعتزلة؛ لبناء أمر المعرفة على العقل عند الفريقين، وأما الذي بَلَغَتْهُ الدعوة، فغَيَّرَ أو قَصَّرَ، فكَفَرَ؛ فيقول بتعذيبه مَنْ ذُكِرَ من الفريقين، والنووي ومن معه، فمتى حَصَلَتِ المعرفة والتوحيد لشخص من أهل الفترة؛ نَجَا باتفاق عند من قال بكفاية العقل لقيامه بالواجب، وَعِنْدَ من قال بالشرع واكتفى بدعوة رسول ما لقيامه بالواجب أيضاً إن كان سَمِعَها، وإلا؛ فلعدم وجوبها عليه وَعِنْدَ مَنْ قال بالشرع بشرط أن يكون مُرْسَلاً إليه لعدم وجوبها عليه، سواءٌ سَمِعَ من رسول لم يُرْسَلْ إليه أو لم يسمع، وتبرُّعُه بها لا يضرُّهُ. ومن هنا يَحْصُلُ جَزْمُكَ بأن أبويه ناجيان؛ لما قيل إنهما كانا على الحنيفية دين إبراهيم كما كان على ذلك طائفة من العرب كزَيْدِ بنِ عَمْرو بن نُفَيْلِ ووَرَقَةَ بنِ نَوْفَلَ، وغيرهما في تلك الأيام، فهما - إن شاء تعالى - في أعلى عليين في دار السلام
The summary of what is said: that the one whom the call has not reached, only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila say he is punished, because the matter of knowledge is based on reason for the two groups. As for the one whom the call reached but changed or fell short, he disbelieves; thus the aforementioned groups and al-Nawawi and those with him say he is punished. Whenever knowledge and tawhid occur for a person from the people of interval, he is saved by agreement according to those who say reason suffices for performing the obligation. And according to those who say (it is based on) revelation and suffice with the call of a certain messenger for performing the obligation, also if he heard it; otherwise, due to its not being obligatory on him. And according to those who say (it is based on) revelation conditioned upon being sent to him, due to its not being obligatory on him, whether he heard from a messenger not sent to him or did not hear; and his voluntarily performing it does not harm him. From here you gain certainty that his parents are saved, because it is said that they were upon the Hanifiyya, the religion of Abraham, as a group of Arabs were upon that, like Zayd ibn Amr ibn Nufayl and Waraqa ibn Nawfal, and others in those days. So they—God willing—are in the highest 'Illiyin in the Abode of Peace.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage summarizes the theological positions on the fate of ahl al-fatrah. The key point: only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila punish those whom the call never reached, because they consider reason as a sufficient basis for obligation. Those who received the call but fell into disbelief are punished by all groups, including Nawawi. For a person from ahl al-fatrah who attains knowledge of tawhid, there is agreement on salvation according to three scenarios: (1) if reason suffices, (2) if revelation suffices and he heard a messenger's call (any messenger), (3) if revelation requires being sent to him specifically, but he was not sent to, so it is not obligatory. Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for salvation under the view that revelation suffices with any messenger?
A) The person must hear a messenger
B) The person must be sent to a messenger
C) Reason alone is enough
D) The person must perform all obligations

Key Takeaway
There are three theological bases for saving ahl al-fatrah who attain tawhid: reason, revelation with any messenger, or revelation with a specific messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you were an ahl al-fatrah person. According to the three views, what would be the basis for your salvation? How does this challenge your assumption about the necessity of hearing a specific messenger? Now, reflect: do you treat your own faith as a product of reason, or as a tradition you inherited? This lesson reveals that for some scholars, reason alone suffices. Does your own conviction in tawhid come from rational certainty or mere habit? Commit to verifying the rational basis for your belief in one God today.
?
The passage states that salvation is agreed upon for those who attain tawhid, regardless of the view. But what does it mean to truly 'attain' tawhid? Is your tawhid merely a claim, or does it transform how you live? Consider the phrase 'knowledge and tawhid'—do you have both? If not, what step will you take to move from belief to certainty? Write down one action you will take to deepen your tawhid before tomorrow.
?
Among the three views, one emphasizes that if a person hears a messenger not sent to them, it does not obligate them. This implies that God's judgment is based on what reaches a person. How does this affect your view of people of other faiths or those who have not heard of Islam? But more importantly, you have heard the message. Are you fulfilling the obligations that come with that hearing? What have you neglected that you need to address today?

Certainty of Salvation for the Prophet's Parents
Sanusi concludes that the Prophet's parents are saved, citing their Hanifiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author concludes that the Prophet's parents are saved, based on their adherence to Hanifiyya.

Core Idea
The Prophet's parents are in Paradise because they followed the pure monotheism of Abraham (Hanifiyya).

In the final part of the passage, the author applies the previous discussion to a specific historical case: the parents of Prophet Muhammad. Citing the tradition that they were Hanifs, he declares certainty of their salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'ومن هنا يحصل' to the end, and the translation.
2
Identify the claim
The passage states that you become certain that the Prophet's parents are saved.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reason given is that they were on the Hanifiyya, the religion of Abraham, like other pre-Islamic monotheists.
This places them among ahl al-fatrah who attained tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل ما يقال: إن الذي لم تَبْلُغه دعوة لا يقول بتعذيبه إلا بعض المَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ والمعتزلة؛ لبناء أمر المعرفة على العقل عند الفريقين، وأما الذي بَلَغَتْهُ الدعوة، فغَيَّرَ أو قَصَّرَ، فكَفَرَ؛ فيقول بتعذيبه مَنْ ذُكِرَ من الفريقين، والنووي ومن معه، فمتى حَصَلَتِ المعرفة والتوحيد لشخص من أهل الفترة؛ نَجَا باتفاق عند من قال بكفاية العقل لقيامه بالواجب، وَعِنْدَ من قال بالشرع واكتفى بدعوة رسول ما لقيامه بالواجب أيضاً إن كان سَمِعَها، وإلا؛ فلعدم وجوبها عليه وَعِنْدَ مَنْ قال بالشرع بشرط أن يكون مُرْسَلاً إليه لعدم وجوبها عليه، سواءٌ سَمِعَ من رسول لم يُرْسَلْ إليه أو لم يسمع، وتبرُّعُه بها لا يضرُّهُ. ومن هنا يَحْصُلُ جَزْمُكَ بأن أبويه ناجيان؛ لما قيل إنهما كانا على الحنيفية دين إبراهيم كما كان على ذلك طائفة من العرب كزَيْدِ بنِ عَمْرو بن نُفَيْلِ ووَرَقَةَ بنِ نَوْفَلَ، وغيرهما في تلك الأيام، فهما - إن شاء تعالى - في أعلى عليين في دار السلام
The summary of what is said: that the one whom the call has not reached, only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila say he is punished, because the matter of knowledge is based on reason for the two groups. As for the one whom the call reached but changed or fell short, he disbelieves; thus the aforementioned groups and al-Nawawi and those with him say he is punished. Whenever knowledge and tawhid occur for a person from the people of interval, he is saved by agreement according to those who say reason suffices for performing the obligation. And according to those who say (it is based on) revelation and suffice with the call of a certain messenger for performing the obligation, also if he heard it; otherwise, due to its not being obligatory on him. And according to those who say (it is based on) revelation conditioned upon being sent to him, due to its not being obligatory on him, whether he heard from a messenger not sent to him or did not hear; and his voluntarily performing it does not harm him. From here you gain certainty that his parents are saved, because it is said that they were upon the Hanifiyya, the religion of Abraham, as a group of Arabs were upon that, like Zayd ibn Amr ibn Nufayl and Waraqa ibn Nawfal, and others in those days. So they—God willing—are in the highest 'Illiyin in the Abode of Peace.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage summarizes the theological positions on the fate of ahl al-fatrah. The key point: only some Maturidis and Mu'tazila punish those whom the call never reached, because they consider reason as a sufficient basis for obligation. Those who received the call but fell into disbelief are punished by all groups, including Nawawi. For a person from ahl al-fatrah who attains knowledge of tawhid, there is agreement on salvation according to three scenarios: (1) if reason suffices, (2) if revelation suffices and he heard a messenger's call (any messenger), (3) if revelation requires being sent to him specifically, but he was not sent to, so it is not obligatory. The passage then decisively applies this to the Prophet's parents, citing that they were on the Hanifiyya (pure monotheism) like Zayd ibn Amr and Waraqa. Thus they are in paradise.

The Prophet's Parents Saved as Ahl al-Fatrah
Al-Bajuri argues the Prophet's parents are saved as ahl al-fatrah and cites a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the Prophet's parents are saved as ahl al-fatrah and the evidence cited from a hadith.

Core Idea
The Prophet's parents are saved because they were from ahl al-fatrah and later believed.

This lesson examines Al-Bajuri's argument that the Prophet's parents are among the saved. He bases this on the status of ahl al-fatrah (people who lived in a period without divine revelation) and a hadith about their revival. We will explore the logic and the poetic citation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the conditional structure at the beginning.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The passage starts with a conditional: if ahl al-fatrah are saved, then the Prophet's parents are saved. Understand the logic behind this link.
This relies on the premise that ahl al-fatrah are saved, which is a known position in kalam.
3
Analyze the hadith and poem
The author cites a hadith about the parents being revived after the Prophet's mission. The poem reinforces this and acknowledges the hadith's weakness but affirms God's power.
Consider why the author includes a poem and a marginal note.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت أن أهل الفترة ناجون؛ علمت أن أبويه ناجيان لكونهما من أهل الفترة، بل هما من أهل الإسلام؛ لما روي أن الله تعالى أحياهما بعد بعثة النبي، فآمنا به، ولذا قال بعضهم: حبا الله النبي مزيد فضل على فضل وكان به رؤوفا فأحيا أمه وكذا أباه لإيمان به فضلا منيفا فسلم فالقديم بذا قدير وإن كان الحديث به ضعيفا
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: فضلاً) متعلق بـ(أحيا)، ومبيناً لمعنى زائد.
And if you know that the people of the interregnum (ahl al-fatrah) are saved, you know that his two parents are saved because they are from the people of the interregnum; rather, they are from the people of Islam, because it is narrated that Allah the Exalted revived them after the mission of the Prophet, so they believed in him. Hence some said: 'God gifted the Prophet an increase of grace upon grace, and He was compassionate to him, so He revived his mother and likewise his father for faith in him, an excellent grace. So submit: the Eternal is capable of this, even if the hadith concerning it is weak.' Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'an excellent grace') is attached to (revived) and clarifies an additional meaning.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the salvation of the Prophet's parents. It begins with a conditional: if ahl al-fatrah are saved, then the Prophet's parents are saved because they were from that period. The author then claims they are actually among the people of Islam, citing a hadith that Allah revived them after the Prophet's mission so they believed. A poem is quoted affirming this divine favor. The poem's last line acknowledges the hadith may be weak but asserts God's power. Finally, al-Ajhuri's marginal note explains that the word 'an excellent grace' (فَضْلاً) is grammatically attached to the verb 'revived' and indicates an additional meaning.

The Nature and Role of Angels According to al-Bajuri
Al-Bajuri defines angels, their nature, functions, and fate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic conception of angels as luminous, bodily beings with specific functions and eschatological roles, as defined by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Angels are luminous bodily messengers of Allah with unique attributes and a defined fate.

In this passage from his commentary on Umm al-Barahin, al-Bajuri provides a comprehensive definition of angels, covering their nature as luminous bodies, their role as intermediaries, and their distinctive attributes that set them apart from humans and jinn. He also addresses their eschatological fate, including resurrection in Paradise and the exceptional death of certain archangels. This lesson unpacks these details to give you a clear picture of angelology in Sunni kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the full scope of al-Bajuri's definition.
The passage includes marginal notes that clarify key terms.
2
Identify the nature of angels
Note that they are described as 'luminous subtle bodies' (ajsam nuraniyya latifa).
This counters the idea of purely spiritual beings.
3
List their attributes
They do not eat, drink, marry, procreate, sleep; their deeds are not recorded; they are truthful messengers.
These attributes distinguish them from humans.
4
Understand their eschatological fate
They will be resurrected, enter Paradise, and die only at the first trumpet blast, except for certain archangels.
The last to die is the Angel of Death.
5
Review the marginal notes
The notes define 'sufara'' as intermediaries, 'taqdis' as declaring Allah free from imperfection, and name the four archangels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ) وهم أجسام نورانيَّةً لطيفة، بالِغُونَ في الكثرة إلى حَدٍ لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى سُفَرَاءُ الله تعالى، صادقون فيما أخبروا به عنه تعالى، لا يأكلون ولا يشربون ولا يتناكحون ولا يتوالدون ولا ينامون ولا تُكتب أعمالهم، ولا يُحاسبون، ويُخشَرون مع الإنس والجن، ويدخلون الجنة، ويتنعمون فيها بما شاء الله. وقيل: يكونون فيها لحالتهم في الدنيا، فلا يأكلون ولا يشربون، بل يُلْهَمُون التسبيحَ والتقديس، فيَجِدُون فيه لَذَّةً كما يَجِدُ أهل الجنة من لذة الطعام والشراب، ويجوزُ عليهم الموتُ لكن لا يَمُوتُ أحد منهم قبل النفخة الأولى، بل بها، إلا حَمَلَةَ العرشِ والرُّؤساء الأربعةَ، فإنهم يموتون بعدها ، وآخرُ من يموت مَلَكُ الموت. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري.
(قوله: سُفَرَاءُ الله أي: الواسطة بينه وبين خلقه المتصرفون بحسب ما يؤذن لهم اهـ من "حاشية الهدهدي".
:(قوله: وَالتَّقْدِيسَ) أي: تنزيهه تعالى عما لا يليق به فهو مرادف للتسبيح (قوله: وَالرُّوَسَاءَ الْأَرْبَعَةَ هم جبرائيل وميكائيل وإسرافيل وعزرائيل.
(His saying: 'and the angels') and they are luminous subtle bodies, reaching in multitude to a limit that none knows except Allah ta'ala, messengers (sufara') of Allah ta'ala, truthful in what they report from Him ta'ala, they do not eat, nor drink, nor marry, nor procreate, nor sleep, nor are their deeds recorded, nor are they taken to account. They will be gathered with humans and jinn, enter Paradise, and enjoy therein whatever Allah wills. And it is said: they will be in it as they were in the world, so they do not eat nor drink, but are inspired with glorification (tasbih) and sanctification (taqdis), and they experience pleasure in that as the people of Paradise experience pleasure in food and drink. Death is possible for them, but none die before the first trumpet blast; rather they die by it, except the bearers of the Throne and the four chiefs, for they die after it, and the last to die is the Angel of Death. Marginal note of al-'Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'messengers of Allah' meaning: the intermediaries between Him and His creation, who act according to what they are permitted; end quote from 'Hashiyat al-Hadadi'.) (His saying: 'and sanctification' meaning: declaring Him free from what does not befit Him; it is synonymous with glorification. (His saying: 'the four chiefs' they are Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, and 'Azra'il.)
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines angels (al-mala'ika). It begins by stating they are luminous, subtle bodies (ajsam nuraniyya latifa), so numerous that only Allah knows their count. They are 'messengers' (sufara') of Allah, truthful in reporting from Him. They lack physical human activities: they do not eat, drink, marry, procreate, or sleep. Their deeds are not recorded, and they are not taken to account. They will be resurrected along with humans and jinn, enter Paradise, and enjoy it. There is a difference of opinion over their experience in Paradise: some say they remain as they were, that is, they do not eat or drink but are inspired with tasbih and taqdis, finding pleasure therein analogous to human pleasure in food. Death is possible for them, but they die only at the first trumpet blast, except for the bearers of the Throne and the four archangels (Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, 'Azra'il), who die after that blast. The last to die is the Angel of Death ('Azra'il). Marginal notes clarify that 'sufara'' means intermediaries, 'taqdis' is synonymous with tasbih in meaning of declaring Allah free from imperfection, and the 'four chiefs' are named.

أجسام نورانية
سفراء
التسبيح
التقديس
حملة العرش
الرؤساء الأربعة
ملك الموت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The text explicitly describes them as 'luminous subtle bodies', affirming a bodily nature, though subtle and light-based.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Angels are created as luminous bodily beings, act as faithful messengers, and have a specific eschatological fate that includes resurrection and exceptional death for some.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Angels do not eat, drink, or sleep—they are entirely consumed by service to Allah. You are wrapped up in these needs. How can you turn your next meal, your next rest, into an act of devotion that draws you nearer to Allah rather than merely satisfying your appetite?
?
The Angel of Death will be the last angel to die, witnessing the end of all others before his own. You will one day face him alone. Are you living today as if that meeting could be tomorrow? Write down one specific change you will make in your routine this week to prepare for that appointment.

The Nature and Attributes of Angels
Al-Bajuri defines angels and lists their attributes and eschatological details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic definition of angels as luminous bodies, their unique attributes that distinguish them from humans, and their eschatological state including death and resurrection.

Core Idea
Angels are luminous, sinless messengers who neither eat nor sleep, and even they will face death.

Al-Bajuri, in his commentary, provides a concise yet comprehensive description of angels. He begins by defining their essential nature, then lists their distinguishing characteristics that set them apart from humans and jinn. The passage also addresses their role in the afterlife and the exceptional circumstances of their death.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and attributes
Read the first part of the passage focusing on the definition of angels as luminous, subtle bodies and the list of attributes that follow.
Notice the emphasis on their multiplicity, known only to Allah.
2
Identify how angels differ from humans
Note the specific attributes that set angels apart: not eating, drinking, marrying, procreating, sleeping, and not being held accountable for deeds.
This highlights their unique, sinless nature.
3
Examine the afterlife of angels
Read the part about resurrection, entry into Paradise, and the opinion that they continue glorifying instead of eating.
Consider why glorification is presented as a pleasure.
4
Understand the death of angels
Read about when angels die (first trumpet blast) and the exceptions: the Throne-bearers and four chiefs die after, with Angel of Death last.
This shows that even angels are subject to death except as noted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ) وهم أجسام نورانيَّةً لطيفة، بالِغُونَ في الكثرة إلى حَدٍ لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى سُفَرَاءُ الله تعالى، صادقون فيما أخبروا به عنه تعالى، لا يأكلون ولا يشربون ولا يتناكحون ولا يتوالدون ولا ينامون ولا تُكتب أعمالهم، ولا يُحاسبون، ويُخشَرون مع الإنس والجن، ويدخلون الجنة، ويتنعمون فيها بما شاء الله. وقيل: يكونون فيها لحالتهم في الدنيا، فلا يأكلون ولا يشربون، بل يُلْهَمُون التسبيحَ والتقديس، فيَجِدُون فيه لَذَّةً كما يَجِدُ أهل الجنة من لذة الطعام والشراب، ويجوزُ عليهم الموتُ لكن لا يَمُوتُ أحد منهم قبل النفخة الأولى، بل بها، إلا حَمَلَةَ العرشِ والرُّؤساء الأربعةَ، فإنهم يموتون بعدها ، وآخرُ من يموت مَلَكُ الموت. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري.
(قوله: سُفَرَاءُ الله أي: الواسطة بينه وبين خلقه المتصرفون بحسب ما يؤذن لهم اهـ من "حاشية الهدهدي".
(قوله: وَالتَّقْدِيسَ) أي: تنزيهه تعالى عما لا يليق به فهو مرادف للتسبيح (قوله: وَالرُّوَسَاءَ الْأَرْبَعَةَ هم جبرائيل وميكائيل وإسرافيل وعزرائيل.
(His saying: and the angels) They are luminous, subtle bodies, reaching in multiplicity to a limit known only to Allah, messengers of Allah, truthful in what they report about Him, they do not eat, drink, marry, procreate, sleep, nor are their deeds recorded, nor are they held accountable. They will be resurrected with humans and jinn, enter Paradise, and enjoy whatever Allah wills. And it is said: they will be in Paradise as in their worldly state, so they will not eat or drink, but will be inspired with glorification and sanctification, finding pleasure in that as the people of Paradise find pleasure in food and drink. Death is possible for them, but none of them dies before the first blast, rather by it, except the bearers of the Throne and the four chiefs, for they die after it, and the last to die is the Angel of Death. Marginal note of 'Allamah Ahmad al-Ujhuri. (His saying: messengers of Allah) meaning the intermediaries between Him and His creation, who act according to what they are permitted. End. From Hashiyat al-Hadadi. (His saying: and sanctification) meaning exalting Him above what is not befitting, so it is synonymous with glorification. (His saying: and the four chiefs) they are Jibril, Mikail, Israfil, and Azrail.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines angels initially as luminous, subtle bodies, extremely numerous. They are messengers, truthful. They do not have physical needs like eating, drinking, marriage, or sleep. Their deeds are not recorded and they are not held accountable, meaning they are not subject to divine reckoning. Then it discusses their afterlife: they will be resurrected with humans and jinn, enter Paradise, and enjoy. There is an opinion that in Paradise they remain as in this world, not eating or drinking, but instead finding pleasure in glorification. Then it discusses death: angels can die, but only at the first trumpet blast, except the bearers of the Throne and the four chief angels (Jibril, Mikail, Israfil, Azrail) who die after that. The Angel of Death is the last to die. The marginal notes explain 'messengers' as intermediaries, 'sanctification' as exalting Allah, and identify the four chiefs.

سفراء
تسبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels have physical needs like eating, drinking, and sleeping.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that angels do not eat, drink, sleep, marry, or procreate, and are not held accountable—they are sinless, spiritual beings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as an attribute of angels in this passage?
A) They do not eat or drink.
B) They are held accountable for their deeds.
C) They are luminous bodies.
D) They can die.

Key Takeaway
Angels are luminous, sinless beings created by Allah, unique in their attributes and subject to death at the trumpet blast, with exceptions for the Throne-bearers and four chiefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Angels find their ultimate pleasure in glorifying and sanctifying Allah. Do you find genuine delight in remembering Allah, or is it a chore? What specific change can you make today to turn your worship into a source of joy rather than a routine?
?
The angels are sinless and their deeds are not even recorded. Yet you and I are accountable for every action. How does this realization affect the way you view your own slips and sins? Will you commit to seeking forgiveness for one specific sin you've been neglecting today?
?
Even the Angel of Death will die. Your own death is certain but its timing is unknown. If you were to die today, what is one unfinished duty towards Allah or towards others that you would regret? Write it down and take a concrete step to address it within the next 24 hours.

Refutation of the Story of Harut and Marut
Al-Bajuri refutes the story of Harut and Marut and affirms the correct belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the story of Harut and Marut as commonly narrated is rejected by Sunni scholars and what the correct theological interpretation is.

Core Idea
The story of Harut and Marut is unreliable and is explained as a warning, not a disobedience.

This lesson addresses a potential challenge to the doctrine of angelic sinlessness. The famous story of Harut and Marut appears to suggest that angels disobeyed Allah. Al-Bajuri refutes this narrative on multiple grounds: its weak transmission from Isra'iliyyat, its true purpose as a warning to distinguish magic from miracles, and an alternative interpretation that they were righteous humans called angels metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how Al-Bajuri structures his refutation: first restating angelic obedience, then addressing the objection.
2
Understand the source criticism
Al-Bajuri argues that the story comes from Isra'iliyyat (Jewish/Christian sources) and no authentic hadith supports it. Reflect on why this is a strong refutation.
The exegetes (mufassirun) also reject it as unauthentic.
3
Grasp the correct interpretation
Even if the story is accepted, the teaching of magic was not for practice but to warn and to help distinguish miracles from magic. This preserves the sinlessness of angels.
This interpretation was necessary because sorcery had increased due to devils, causing confusion.
4
Consider the alternative opinion
Al-Bajuri mentions that some scholars say Harut and Marut were righteous men, not angels, and were called angels metaphorically. This also avoids the problem.
Both interpretations uphold angelic sinlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يَعْصُون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون ولا ينافي ذلك ما يُنْقَلُ عن هَارُوتَ ومارُوتَ؛ لأنه إنما ينقلُهُ المؤرخون عن الإسرائيليات - أي: كتب اليهود والنصارى ، ولم يَصِحَ فيه خبرٌ كما قاله المفسرون، وما يَذْكُرُه كَذَبَةُ المؤرخين من أنهما عوقبا ومُسِخَا؛ كَذِبٌ ،وزورٌ ، ولا يجوز اعتقاده، بل الذي يجب اعتقاده أن تعليمهما السحر لم يكن لأجل العمل به، بل للتحذير ولِيَظْهَرَ الفرقُ بينه وبين المعجزة، فإنه قد وَقَعَ أن السَّحَرَةَ كَثُروا بسبب استراق الشياطين السمع، وتعليمهم إياهم، فظنَّ الجَهَلَةُ أن معجزاتِ الأنبياء سِحْرٌ ، فأنزلهما الله ليعلما الناس كيفية السحر ليَظْهَرَ لهم الفرقُ بينه وبينها. هذا كلُّه بناءً على أنهما كانا ملكين. وقيل: إنهما كانا رَجُلَين صالحين، وسمّيا ملكين لصلاحهما.
They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them, and they do what they are commanded. This is not contradicted by what is narrated about Harut and Marut; because it is only narrated by historians from the Isra'iliyyat – i.e., the books of the Jews and Christians – and no authentic report has been established concerning it, as the exegetes have said. And what the lying historians mention about them being punished and transformed (into something else) is a lie and falsehood, and it is not permissible to believe it. Rather, what must be believed is that their teaching of magic was not for the purpose of practicing it, but to warn and to make the distinction between it and the miracle apparent. For it happened that sorcerers increased due to the devils' eavesdropping and teaching them, so the ignorant thought that the miracles of the prophets were magic. So Allah sent them down to teach people the nature of magic to make apparent to them the difference between it and miracles. All of this is based on the premise that they were angels. And it is said that they were two righteous men, and they were called angels because of their righteousness.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the sinlessness of angels. The author first restates that angels do not disobey Allah. Then he addresses the famous story of Harut and Marut, which seems to suggest angels disobeyed. He refutes it on two grounds: first, the story comes from Isra'iliyyat (Jewish/Christian sources) and is not authentic. Second, even if taken as true, the correct interpretation is that they taught magic not to practice it but to warn people and to help distinguish miracles from magic. This was necessary because the increase in magic due to devils caused confusion. The author then notes that this interpretation assumes they were angels, but there is also an opinion that they were righteous men called angels figuratively.

Key Terms
هاروت وماروت
سحر
معجزة
إسرائيليات
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people believe that the story of Harut and Marut is a factual historical event showing that angels can disobey Allah and be punished.
Correction: The story is not authentically transmitted from Islamic sources but comes from Isra'iliyyat. Even if accepted, the angels taught magic for a wise purpose (warning and distinguishing miracles), not out of disobedience. An alternative view holds they were righteous humans.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct belief about Harut and Marut's teaching of magic?
A) They taught magic to practice it themselves.
B) They taught magic to warn people and distinguish it from miracles.
C) They were punished and transformed into something else.
D) They were not angels but humans.

Key Takeaway
The story of Harut and Marut is either unauthentic or, if accepted, must be interpreted in a way that preserves the sinlessness of angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a story about Islam that seems sensational or surprising, do you immediately question its authenticity, or do you accept it because it's interesting? What would it take for you to reject a popular tale if it contradicts what you know Allah must be like?
?
Have you ever been tempted to believe something about Allah or His angels based on a story you heard, even if it conflicted with His perfection? How can you train yourself to submit to sound theology over captivating narratives?
?
This lesson teaches that even if a story is famous, it may be false. Is there a belief you hold that you have never checked against the Quran and authentic Sunnah? Will you commit to verifying one belief this week?

Belief in Specific Angels and Guardians
Al-Bajuri explains the obligation to believe in specific angels and guardians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of detailed belief in the angels specifically mentioned in revelation, and the role of guardian angels.

Core Idea
Some angels require detailed faith; others are believed in generally.

Al-Bajuri explains that while angels are innumerable and general belief suffices for most, revelation singles out certain angels by name or type, demanding detailed faith. This lesson clarifies which angels fall under detailed belief and introduces the guardians appointed to protect every person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the main point about general vs. detailed belief.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'general' and 'detailed' belief.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between angels known by name and those known by type. List the named angels and the types mentioned.
Named angels are eight; types include bearers of the Throne and guardians.
3
Reflect on the guardians
Consider the role of guardian angels (hafazah) and the Quranic evidence provided. How does this concept relate to divine protection?
The verse (13:11) emphasizes continuous protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عرفت أنهم بَالِغُون في الكثرة إلى حَدٍ لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى، فيجب الإيمان بهم على الإجمال إلا من وَرَدَ تعيينه باسمه المخصوص أو نوعه، فيجب الإيمانُ بهم تفصيلاً، فالأول: كجِبْرِيلَ وميكائيل وإسرافيل وعِزْرَائِيلَ ومُنْكَرٍ ونكير ورِضْوَان وَمَالِكٍ، والثاني: كحَمَلَةِ العرش والحَفَظَةِ: وهم ملائكةٌ مُوَكَّلون بحفظ العبد، قال تعالى: لَهُ مُعَقِّبَتُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَمِنْ خَلْفِهِ يَحْفَظُونَهُ مِنْ أَمْرِ الله ) (1).
And you have known that they are extremely numerous to a limit known only to Allah. So it is obligatory to believe in them in general, except for those whose specification has come by their particular name or type. So belief in them is obligatory in detail. The first category: like Jibril, Mikail, Israfil, Azrail, Munkar, Nakir, Ridwan, and Malik. The second category: like the bearers of the Throne and the guardians: they are angels entrusted with the preservation of the servant, as Allah said: 'For him are guardian angels before him and behind him, preserving him by the command of Allah.' (Surah al-Ra'd: 11)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage summarizes the obligation of belief in angels. Since their number is immense, faith in general is sufficient. However, for those angels whose names or types have been reported in revelation, belief must be in detail. The author lists eight named angels: Jibril, Mikail, Israfil, Azrail (the four archangels), Munkar and Nakir (the angels of the grave), Ridwan (the keeper of Paradise), and Malik (the keeper of Hell). Then he mentions two types: the bearers of the Throne and the guardians (hafazah), who are angels appointed to protect every servant. He quotes the Quranic verse (13:11) about guardian angels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must believe in every single angel individually.
Correction: The passage teaches that general belief suffices for most angels; detailed belief is only required for those specifically mentioned in revelation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a category requiring detailed belief?
A) Angels named individually like Jibril and Mikail.
B) Types of angels like the bearers of the Throne.
C) All angels without exception.
D) Guardians (hafazah).

Key Takeaway
Belief in angels is obligatory in general, but for those specifically named or described in revelation, detailed faith is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you let this knowledge change one habit this week?
?
Belief in angels is part of your iman. But does your heart truly feel the reality of their existence? What would change in your daily life if you constantly remembered that your deeds are recorded and you are protected by angels? Ask yourself honestly: are you living as if these unseen beings are real? Commit to one sincere prayer tonight asking Allah to increase your certainty.

The True Nature of Harut and Marut
Sanusi refutes false stories about Harut and Marut and states the correct belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the story of Harut and Marut is rejected and what the correct Islamic belief about them is.

Core Idea
The story of Harut and Marut comes from unreliable sources; their teaching was for discernment.

Sanusi addresses a common false narrative about two angels named Harut and Marut. He dismisses the story as based on Isra'iliyyat, not authentic Islamic sources. This lesson clarifies the true purpose of their teaching and why believing false reports is forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Notice that the passage starts with a general principle about angels' obedience.
2
Identify the false story and Sanusi's rejection
Find where Sanusi mentions the narration about Harut and Marut and how he dismisses it as Isra'iliyyat and lies.
He says no authentic report exists and it's forbidden to believe.
3
Understand the correct purpose
Sanusi explains that their teaching of sorcery was for warning, not practice, to distinguish miracles from sorcery.
This is the core correct belief.
4
Note the two views on their nature
The passage ends with two possibilities: they were angels or righteous men called angels metaphorically.
Both views are allowed, but the first view is presented as the basis for the explanation.
5
Reflect on the marginal comment
Al-Ajhuri points out a subtlety in the reason for sorcery's increase – the devils' eavesdropping.
Consider why this connection might be considered obscure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يَعْصُون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون ولا ينافي ذلك ما يُنْقَلُ عن هَارُوتَ ومارُوتَ؛ لأنه إنما ينقلُهُ المؤرخون عن الإسرائيليات - أي: كتب اليهود والنصارى ، ولم يَصِحَ فيه خبرٌ كما قاله المفسرون، وما يَذْكُرُه كَذَبَةُ المؤرخين من أنهما عوقبا ومُسِخَا؛ كَذِبٌ ،وزورٌ ، ولا يجوز اعتقاده، بل الذي يجب اعتقاده أن تعليمهما السحر لم يكن لأجل العمل به، بل للتحذير ولِيَظْهَرَ الفرقُ بينه وبين المعجزة، فإنه قد وَقَعَ أن السَّحَرَةَ كَثُروا بسبب استراق الشياطين السمع، وتعليمهم إياهم، فظنَّ الجَهَلَةُ أن معجزاتِ الأنبياء سِحْرٌ ، فأنزلهما الله ليعلما الناس كيفية السحر ليَظْهَرَ لهم الفرقُ بينه وبينها. هذا كلُّه بناءً على أنهما كانا ملكين. وقيل: إنهما كانا رَجُلَين صالحين، وسمّيا ملكين لصلاحهما. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: بِسَبِبٍ اسْتِرَاقِ الشَّيَاطِينِ السَّمْعَ في جعل هذا سبباً لكثرة السحر خفاء.
They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them, and they do what they are commanded. And that is not contradicted by what is narrated about Harut and Marut, because it is only narrated by historians from the Isra'iliyyat – i.e., the books of the Jews and Christians – and no authentic report has reached us regarding it, as the exegetes have said. And what the lying historians mention about them being punished and transformed is false, invalid, and it is not permissible to believe it. Rather, what must be believed is that their teaching of sorcery was not for the purpose of practicing it, but for warning and to make clear the difference between it and miracles. For it happened that sorcerers became numerous due to the devils' eavesdropping on the heavenly discourse and their teaching them, so the ignorant thought that the miracles of the prophets were sorcery. So Allah sent them down to teach people the method of sorcery so that the difference between it and miracles would become clear to them. All this is based on the view that they were two angels. And it is said: they were two righteous men, and they were called angels due to their righteousness. A marginal comment by the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: 'His saying: "due to the devils' eavesdropping on the heavenly discourse" – there is concealment in making this a cause for the increase of sorcery.'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is both a refutation and a definition. It refutes false stories about Harut and Marut and defines the correct belief about them. The argument follows: first, it states that angels do not disobey Allah, then it addresses the narration about Harut and Marut, dismissing it as based on unreliable Isra'iliyyat (Jewish/Christian sources) with no authentic report. It then corrects the belief: they were sent to teach sorcery not for practice but to distinguish miracles from sorcery, because sorcery increased due to devils' eavesdropping. The passage ends by noting two views on their nature (angels or righteous men). The marginal comment by al-Ajhuri points out a subtlety: making the devils' eavesdropping a cause for sorcery's increase is obscure.

Key Terms
هاروت وماروت
السحر
المعجزة
الإسرائيليات
استراق السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harut and Marut were punished for teaching sorcery.
Correction: The passage clarifies they were not punished; they taught to distinguish sorcery from miracles.

Checkpoint
What is the correct belief regarding Harut and Marut according to Sanusi?
A) They were punished for teaching sorcery.
B) They taught sorcery for practice.
C) They taught sorcery to distinguish it from miracles.
D) They were righteous men, not angels.

Key Takeaway
The correct belief is that Harut and Marut, whether angels or righteous men, taught sorcery to warn people and to differentiate it from prophetic miracles.

Belief in Angels: General and Specific
Sanusi classifies belief in angels into general and specific, listing named angels and types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two levels of belief in angels: general belief in all angels and specific belief in those named in revelation.

Core Idea
Belief in angels requires both general acceptance and specific knowledge of named ones.

Sanusi now turns to the sam'iyyat, matters known through revelation. He establishes that while believing in all angels is obligatory in general, revelation singles out certain angels and types for detailed belief. This passage clarifies which angels we must know by name and which by description, grounding the obligation in divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a general rule, an exception, a list, then a verse.
The passage includes a parenthetical commentary from the translator; focus on Sanusi's original words first.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides belief in angels into general (ijmal) and specific (tafsil). Find where each is mentioned and what triggers specific belief.
The exception is 'except for those whose specific name or type has come'.
3
List the named angels and types
Note the eight angels listed individually, then the two types: bearers of the Throne and guardian angels. Reflect on why these are singled out.
Guardian angels are linked to Qur'an 13:11, which Sanusi then explains.
4
Examine the Qur'anic proof
Read the verse and the linguistic note on 'min' meaning 'bi' (causation). Consider how this verse supports the existence and function of guardian angels.
The commentary shows how careful tafsir clarifies the role of angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عرفت أنهم بَالِغُون في الكثرة إلى حَدٍ لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى، فيجب الإيمان بهم على الإجمال إلا من وَرَدَ تعيينه باسمه المخصوص أو نوعه، فيجب الإيمانُ بهم تفصيلاً، فالأول: كجِبْرِيلَ وميكائيل وإسرافيل وعِزْرَائِيلَ ومُنْكَرٍ ونكير ورِضْوَان وَمَالِكٍ، والثاني: كحَمَلَةِ العرش والحَفَظَةِ: وهم ملائكةٌ مُوَكَّلون بحفظ العبد، قال تعالى: لَهُ مُعَقِّبَتُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَمِنْ خَلْفِهِ يَحْفَظُونَهُ مِنْ أَمْرِ الله ) (1) . (قوله: (يَحْفَظُونَهُ مِنْ أَمْرِ الله في البيضاوي عبارة طويلة من جملتها: أن (من) بمعنى الباء، والمعنى حينئذ : أنهم يحفظونه بسبب أمر الله إياهم بذلك.
And you have known that they [angels] are extremely numerous to a degree that none knows except Allah Almighty. So it is obligatory to believe in them in general, except for those whose specific name or type has come, so it is obligatory to believe in them in detail. The first: like Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, 'Azra'il, Munkar, Nakir, Ridwan, and Malik. The second: like the bearers of the Throne and the Guardian Angels, who are angels entrusted with guarding the servant. Allah said: 'For him are successive angels before him and behind him, guarding him by the command of Allah.' (Qur'an 13:11). Commentator's note: 'His saying: "guarding him by the command of Allah" – in al-Baydawi there is a long statement, part of which is that "min" (by) has the meaning of "bi" (with/by), and the meaning then is that they guard him because of Allah's command to them to do so.'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It classifies belief in angels into general belief (obligatory for all) and specific belief (obligatory for those named). It then lists the named angels and the named types. The passage concludes with a Qur'anic verse and a linguistic clarification from the commentary on the verse, explaining the preposition 'min' as meaning 'bi' indicating causation.

الإجمال

Correction: The passage teaches that while general belief is sufficient for most angels, detailed belief in those named in revelation (like Jibril and Mika'il) is also obligatory.

The Number of Guardian Angels and Their Scope
Al-Ajhuri reports a claim and a debate about guardian angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reported number of guardian angels for humans and the debate about whether jinn and angels have guardians.

Core Idea
Guardian angels are assigned to humans; opinions differ about jinn.

This lesson explores a specific tradition about the number of guardian angels assigned to each human from conception to death, and the scholarly discussion about whether such guardians are also assigned to jinn and angels. The passage reports two distinct positions, giving us insight into how classical scholars debated unseen matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two different reports: one from Ibn 'Atiyya about humans, and another from al-Jazuli about jinn and angels.
2
Identify the two claims
Find the claim about humans (four hundred angels) and the discussion about jinn and angels. Note who made each claim and what the final position was.
The first claim is attributed to Ibn 'Atiyya; the second is al-Jazuli's reasoning.
3
Understand al-Jazuli's hesitation
Al-Jazuli first hesitated whether jinn and angels have guardians, then affirmed for jinn and denied for angels. Think about why he might have made that distinction.
The passage does not give reasons, so any reasoning is speculative.

The Recording Angels: Their Role, Number, and Location
Al-Bajuri describes the function, number opinions, and location of the recording angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the function of the recording angels, the differing opinions on their number and location, and their post-mortem role.

Core Idea
Recording angels write every action, thought, and intention—good or evil.

Al-Bajuri now turns to the 'scribes' (al-kutabah), the angels tasked with recording human deeds. This passage clarifies their comprehensive function, addresses a common question about their presence during intimate moments, and presents multiple scholarly views on how many there are and where they are located on the body. Pay attention to the five opinions on their location, as this reflects the diversity of transmitted traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's words settle before analysis.
The passage is dense with multiple sub-topics.
2
Understand the function of the recording angels

The angels are entrusted with comprehensive recording.

The well-known view is two angels per day and night. Another view holds that two angels accompany a person throughout life and then act at the grave.
The second view includes a post-mortem role.
4

It is said: the hair below his lower lip. It is said: his molars. And it is reported in some traditions – as al-'Allama al-Laqani said – that some good deeds are written by other than these two angels.

Multiple Interpretations of 'Min Amr Allah'
Al-Anbabi provides multiple interpretations of the verse about guardian angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of the phrase 'guarding him by the command of Allah' as provided by Al-Anbabi.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic phrase carries layers of meaning from grammatical to theological.

Al-Anbabi, in his notes, unpacks a key Qur'anic phrase from the verse about guardian angels. He presents several ways to understand 'min amr Allah,' showing the richness of Islamic exegesis. In this lesson, we will explore these interpretations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the different interpretations listed.
The passage begins with the phrase 'تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي' indicating it's from his notes.
2
Identify the interpretations
List each distinct interpretation Al-Anbabi provides for 'guarding him by the command of Allah'. There are at least four.
Look for the phrases 'أي من بأسه', 'أو يحفظونه من المضار', 'أو يراقبون أحواله', and the grammatical notes on 'من'.
3
Analyze the grammatical note

This shows the importance of Arabic grammar in interpreting Quranic verses.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why multiple interpretations exist. Does this contradict the verse or enrich it?
Scholars often present multiple valid interpretations without contradiction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (الجماعات مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَمِنْ خَلْفِهِ ) أي من جميع جوانبه، أو ما قَدَّمَ وأَخْر من الأعمال يحفَظُونَهُ مِنْ أَمْرِ اللَّهِ :أي من بأسه حين أذْنَبَ بالاستعمال والاستغفار، أو يحفظونه من المضار، أو يراقبون أحواله من أجل أَمْرِ الله وقيل : (من) بمعنى الباء، وقيل: مِنْ أَمْرِ الله صفةٌ ثانية لمعقبات.
Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: 'The groups from before him and behind him' – meaning from all his sides, or what he has advanced and delayed of deeds – 'guarding him by the command of Allah' – meaning from His punishment when he sins, by means of seeking forgiveness? Or they guard him from harms, or they watch over his states for the sake of Allah's command. And it is said: 'min' (by) has the meaning of 'bi' (with/by). And it is said: 'min amr Allah' is a second adjective for 'mu'aqqibat' (successive angels).
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical analysis. It provides multiple interpretations of the Qur'anic phrase 'guarding him by the command of Allah'. The interpretations include: guarding from Allah's punishment when sinning and repenting, guarding from harms, watching over states for Allah's sake, and a grammatical analysis of the preposition 'min'.

Angels: Keepers and Recorders of Deeds
Discusses guardian and recording angels, their numbers, and locations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic teachings about guardian and recording angels, including their numbers, functions, and the scholarly diversity on their locations.

Core Idea
Angels accompany every human from womb to grave, recording all deeds and guarding.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary discusses two kinds of angels: those who guard and those who record deeds. It presents various scholarly opinions about their numbers and even their physical placement on the body, illustrating that while belief in angels is essential, many details are not fixed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from guardian angels to recording angels and the various opinions.
The passage begins with a report from al-Abi and ends with a tradition from al-Laqani.
2
Identify the two angelic categories
The passage mentions two groups: 'حفظة' (guardians) and 'كتبة' (scribes/recorders). Understand the role of each.
Guardians are mentioned briefly; the focus is on the recording angels.
3
Examine the opinions on numbers
Note that there are multiple views: 400 angels from conception to death, two per day and night, or two permanent angels. Consider what this diversity teaches.
The well-known view is two per day and night, but other opinions exist.
4
Understand what the scribes record
The recording angels write down everything: words, actions, beliefs, passing thoughts, resolves, approvals – both good and evil. Their departure during intercourse does not prevent recording.
This emphasizes the comprehensiveness of divine record-keeping.
5
Reflect on the location of the angels
There are five opinions about where the two angels reside on the body: shoulders, chin, lips, mustache, or molars. List them and note that this shows scholarly difference.
The passage does not endorse one view, leaving such details open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الأبي: أنه يَحْفَظُ لابن عطية أن كل آدمي يُوكل به من حين وقوعه في الرحم إلى موته أربعمائة مَلَك، وتردَّد الجزُولي هل للجن والملائكة حَفَظَةٌ أو لا؟ ثم جَزَمَ بأن للجن حفظةً، واستبعد القول بذلك في الملائكة. قال الأجهوري: ولم أقف عليه في الجن لغيره. وكالكتبة وهم ملائكة موكَّلون بكتابة ما يَصْدُرُ عن المكلف قولاً، أو فعلاً، أو اعتقاداً، أو هَمّاً، أو عَزْماً، أو تقريراً خيراً أو شراً، ومفارقتهم عند نحو الجماع لا تَمْنَعُ من كتبهم ما يصدُرُ عنه حينئذ، والمشهور أن لكلّ يوم وليلة ملكين. وقيل: هما ملكان فقط يلزمانه ما دام حياً، فإذا مات قاما على قبره يُسبّحان ويهللان ويكتران إلى يوم القيامة إن كان مؤمناً، ويلعنانه إلى يوم القيامة إن كان كافراً. واختلف في محلّهما من المكلف على خمسة أقوال، فقيل: عاتِقاه. وقيل: ذَقْتُه. وقيل: شَفَتَاه. وقيل : عَنْفَقَتُه . وقيل : نَاجِذَاه. وورد في بعض الآثار - كما قاله العلامة اللقاني: أن بعض الخيرات يكتبها غير هذين الملكين.
And al-Abi mentioned: that he preserves from Ibn Atiyyah that every human is entrusted from the time of his falling into the womb until his death with four hundred angels. And al-Jazuli hesitated whether jinn and angels have guardians or not? Then he affirmed that jinn have guardians, and he deemed the statement regarding that concerning angels unlikely. Al-Ajhuri said: I have not come across it regarding jinn from other than him. And like the scribes (katabah) – they are angels entrusted with writing what issues from the accountable person, whether statement, action, belief, passing thought, resolve, or approval, good or evil. Their departure at times like intercourse does not prevent them from writing what issues from him then. The well-known (view) is that for every day and night there are two angels. And it is said: they are only two angels who accompany him as long as he lives, and when he dies they stand at his grave glorifying (Allah), declaring His oneness, and multiplying (good deeds) until the Day of Resurrection if he is a believer, and they curse him until the Day of Resurrection if he is a disbeliever. And they differed concerning their location on the accountable person according to five opinions: it is said: his two shoulders; it is said: his chin; it is said: his two lips; it is said: his mustache; it is said: his two molars. And it is related in some traditions – as al-Allama al-Laqani said: that some good deeds are written by other than these two angels.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage discusses two categories of angels: the guardian angels (hafazah) and the recording angels (katabah). It begins by citing al-Abi, who reports from Ibn Atiyyah that every human is assigned four hundred angels from conception to death. Then it notes al-Jazuli's hesitation about whether jinn and angels themselves have guardian angels; he concluded that jinn do have guardians but considered it unlikely for angels. Al-Ajhuri remarks that he found no other source for jinn having guardians. The passage then describes the recording angels (katabah) who write all deeds—words, actions, beliefs, passing thoughts, resolutions, and approvals—regardless of whether the person is engaging in intimate relations; their departure at such times does not prevent recording. The well-known view is that there are two angels per day and night, but some say it is the same two angels who accompany a person throughout life and then at the grave either glorify God or curse the person depending on their faith. Finally, the passage lists five opinions on where these two angels are located on the body: the shoulders, the chin, the lips, the mustache, or the molars. It concludes by quoting al-Laqani that some traditions indicate that certain good deeds are recorded by angels other than these two. The passage thus presents multiple scholarly opinions without insisting on one, showing that these details are not essential to faith.

Key Takeaway

The Heavenly Books: Number and Required Belief
Discusses the number of revealed books and the correct belief regarding them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Islamic belief regarding the number of revealed books and the obligation to know the four major books in detail.

Core Idea
Believe in all revealed books generally; know the four major ones specifically.

The heavenly books are a fundamental article of faith. But how many books did Allah reveal? Scholars have different opinions on the exact count. This lesson presents those views and clarifies the required belief: we must affirm that Allah sent down books in general, while specifically recognizing the Torah, Psalms, Gospel, and Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from al-Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin carefully. Notice how he defines 'heavenly books' and then presents various opinions on their number.
Pay attention to the key terms: الكتب السماوية, الصحف, etc.
2
Examine the different opinions
The passage lists three well-known opinions: 104 books, 114 books (with two variations). Observe the differences in the numbers of scrolls attributed to prophets.
These numbers are transmitted reports, not matters of belief.
3
Understand the correct position (al-tahqiq)
The sound view is to refrain from specifying a number. We must believe generally that Allah revealed books, but the four major books must be known individually.
This aligns with Islamic theological restraint.
4
Review the marginal commentary
Al-Ajhuri clarifies two phrases: 'in the tablets' (like the Torah) and 'upon the tongue of an angel' (Jibril, like the Qur'an). This explains the modes of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي (قوله: وَالْكُتُبِ السَّمَاوِيَّةِ) أي: المنزلة من السماء في الألواح، أو على لسان مَلَكِ، والمراد بها : ما يَشْمَلُ الصُّحُفَ، وقد اشتهر أنها مائة وأربعة: صُحُفُ شِيثَ ستون، وصُحُفُ إبراهيم ثلاثون، وصحف موسى قبل التوراة عشرة، والكتب الأربعةُ : التوراة لموسى والزَّبُورُ لداود، والإنجيل لعيسى، والفرقان لسيدنا محمد كذا نَقَلَ بعضُ شُرَّاح الأربعين عن الخطيب. وقيل: صُحُفُ شيت خمسون، وصحف إدريس ثلاثون، وصحف إبراهيم وموسى عشرون بالسوية، والكتب الأربعةُ وقيل: إنها مائة وأربعة عشر: صحف شيث خمسون، وصحف إدريس ثلاثون، وصحف إبراهيم عشرون واختلف في عشرة فقيل لآدم، وقيل: لموسى والكتب الأربعة. وهذا القولُ ما نَصَّ عليه التَّتَائِيُّ) في "شرح الرسالة" حيث قال: فائدة: الكتبُ المُنْزَلَةُ من السماء مائة وأربعة عشر إلى آخر ما تقدم. هذا، والتحقيق: الإمساك عن حَصْرِها في عددٍ، فيجب اعتقاد أن الله أنزل كتباً من السماء على الإجمال، نعم الكتب الأربعةُ يجب معرفتها تفصيلاً.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: فِي الْأَلْوَاحِ) كالتوراة. (وقوله : أو عَلَى لِسَانِ مَلَكِ) أي: جبريل كالقرآن.
The text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abd Allah al-Sanusi: (His statement: 'and the heavenly books') means: those sent down from heaven in tablets, or upon the tongue of an angel. And what is intended by them is that which includes the scrolls (suhuf). And it has become well-known that they are one hundred and four: the scrolls of Seth, sixty; the scrolls of Abraham, thirty; the scrolls of Moses before the Torah, ten; and the four books: the Torah for Moses, the Psalms for David, the Gospel for Jesus, and the Criterion (al-Furqan) for our master Muhammad – thus some commentators of al-Arba'in have transmitted from al-Khatib. And it is said: the scrolls of Seth are fifty, the scrolls of Idris are thirty, and the scrolls of Abraham and Moses are twenty equally, and the four books. And it is said: they are one hundred and fourteen: the scrolls of Seth are fifty, the scrolls of Idris are thirty, the scrolls of Abraham are twenty, and there is disagreement about ten: some say for Adam, some say for Moses, and the four books. And this is the statement that al-Tata'i stated in his Sharh al-Risala, where he said: A benefit: the books sent down from heaven are one hundred and fourteen – to the end of what preceded. This, and the correct view is: to refrain from restricting them to a number, so it is obligatory to believe that Allah sent down books from heaven in general. Yes, the four books must be known in detail.
The marginal commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'in the tablets') like the Torah. (And his statement: 'or upon the tongue of an angel') i.e., Jibril, like the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the heavenly books. It begins by glossing al-Sanusi's phrase 'the heavenly books' (al-kutub al-samawiyya), explaining that they are sent down from heaven either in tablets (alwah) or upon the tongue of an angel (like Jibril for the Qur'an). The term includes scrolls (suhuf) as well as books. The passage then presents three well-known opinions about the total number of revealed scriptures. The first opinion states there are 104: Seth's 60 scrolls, Abraham's 30, Moses' 10 before the Torah, plus the four books (Torah, Psalms, Gospel, Qur'an). The second opinion says 114: Seth 50, Idris 30, Abraham and Moses 20 each (total 100?) Actually it says 'twenty equally' (عشرون بالسوية) meaning twenty each for Abraham and Moses? Wait: 'صَحف إبراهيم وموسى عشرون بالسوية' – that could mean twenty for both together, i.e., ten each? But the text is ambiguous. The third opinion also says 114 but with different distribution: Seth 50, Idris 30, Abraham 20, and ten disputed between Adam and Moses. The passage then cites al-Tata'i who explicitly states 114. After presenting these opinions, the passage gives the sound position (al-tahqiq): one should not restrict the number to a specific count; rather, one must believe generally that Allah sent down books. However, the four major books (Torah, Psalms, Gospel, Qur'an) must be known in detail. Finally, the marginal commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies two phrases from the main text: 'in the tablets' refers to something like the Torah (which was written on tablets), and 'upon the tongue of an angel' means specifically Jibril, as in the case of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
الكتب السماوية
الصحف
التوراة
الزبور
الإنجيل
الفرقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must know the exact number of all revealed books.
Correction: The correct view is to believe generally in all revealed books; only the four major books need to be known in detail.

Checkpoint

C) One should not restrict them to a specific number but believe generally.

Key Takeaway
The required belief is to affirm that Allah revealed books in general, while specifically knowing the Torah, Psalms, Gospel, and Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in the Torah, Psalms, and Gospel as revealed books. Have you ever read a single verse from them? If not, does your belief go beyond mere words on a page?
?
The passage teaches that the four books must be known in detail. How well do you truly know the Qur'an? When was the last time you read it with understanding, or do you treat it as a distant symbol?
?
The scrolls of earlier prophets are lost to history. Does that weaken your conviction in their revelation? Or do you hold to belief in the unseen without needing physical proof—and if so, how does that shape your other beliefs?

Definition and Naming of the Last Day
Al-Sanusi defines the Last Day and explains its naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Last Day is called 'the last' and how the two explanations are actually the same.

Core Idea
The Last Day is final because no night follows it.

Al-Sanusi begins his discussion of the Last Day by clarifying its name. The term 'al-yawm al-akhir' might seem straightforward, but the passage explains its precise meaning and resolves a potential confusion. You will see how the two common interpretations converge into one.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the marginal commentary is part of the lesson.
2
Identify the two explanations for the name
The passage gives two reasons why the Last Day is called 'the last'. Find them and note how they are phrased.
The first is explicit, the second is introduced with 'وقيل'.
3
Understand the marginal commentary's resolution
Al-Ajhuri's marginal note states that these two explanations are essentially one opinion. Understand why they are considered the same.
This shows scholarly precision in reconciling apparent differences.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why defining the Last Day as the final day without night matters for eschatology.
It emphasizes the definitive end of this worldly cycle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ الذي هو يوم القيامة، وإنما وُصِفَ بالآخر؛ لأنه آخِرُ الأيَّام. وقيل: لأنه لا ليل بعده. وأوَّلُه من النفخة الثانية. وقيل: من الحشر. وقيل: من الموت ولا نِهَايَةَ له. وقيل: ينتهي بدخول أهل الجنة الجنةَ، ودخول أهل النار النار. والمراد بالنفخة الثانية: نفخة البعث وهو إحياء الأبدان من القبور، وذلك أنه بعد مَوْتِ الخلائق بالنفخة الأولى - وهي نفخةُ الصَّعْق، وبين النفختين أربعون عاماً -)
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: وقيل : لِأَنَّهُ لَا لَيْلَ... إلخ) هو بمعنى ما قبله، فهما قول واحد. (قوله: الصَّعْقِ) بفتح العين؛ من باب تعب كما في "المصباح".
(His statement: 'and the Last Day' which is the Day of Resurrection. And it is described as 'the last' only because it is the last of the days. And it is said: because there is no night after it. And its beginning is from the second trumpet blast. And it is said: from the Gathering (al-hashr). And it is said: from death and it has no end. And it is said: it ends with the entry of the people of Paradise into Paradise and the entry of the people of Hellfire into Hellfire. And what is meant by the second trumpet blast is the blast of resurrection (nafkhat al-ba'th), which is the reviving of bodies from the graves. And that is after the death of creatures by the first blast – which is the blast of the thunderbolt (nafkhat al-sa'q), and between the two blasts is forty years.)
The marginal commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'and it is said: because there is no night... etc.') it has the same meaning as what preceded it, so they are one statement. (His statement: 'al-sa'q') with fathah on the 'ayn; from the pattern of ta'iba as in al-Misbah.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines the Last Day (al-yawm al-akhir) and explores its characteristics. It begins by glossing al-Sanusi's phrase 'the Last Day' as the Day of Resurrection. It explains why it is called 'the last': either because it is the last of days, or because there is no night after it. The passage then presents four opinions on when the Last Day begins: from the second trumpet blast (nafkhat al-ba'th), from the Gathering (al-hashr), from death (with no end), or that it ends with the final abode of Paradise and Hell. The preferred view is that it begins with the second trumpet, which is the blast of resurrection that revives bodies. This occurs after the first blast (nafkhat al-sa'q) which causes death, with a forty-year interval between them. The marginal commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies two points: first, the alternative explanation that the Last Day is called 'last' because there is no night after it is essentially the same as the first explanation (that it is the last of days), so they are a single opinion. Second, he notes the correct vocalization of 'al-sa'q' (the thunderbolt) is with a fatha on the 'ayn, and it follows the pattern of ta'iba as stated in al-Misbah.

Key Terms
اليوم الآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think there are two distinct opinions about why the Last Day is called 'last'.
Correction: The passage shows that both explanations converge to the same meaning, as the marginal commentary explicitly states they are one statement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the two explanations for why the Last Day is called 'the last'?
A) They are contradictory.
B) They are distinct opinions.
C) They are essentially the same.
D) The second is rejected.

Key Takeaway
The Last Day is called 'the last' because it is the final day with no night following, making it the termination of all days.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Last Day is called 'the last' because no night follows it. If you knew with certainty that today is your last day, what would you change about your actions right now? How would you spend the remaining hours?
?
The two explanations for the name are actually one: a day without night. This precision underscores the finality of the Day of Judgment. Are you living as if that day could come at any moment, or are you putting off repentance and good deeds? What is one specific deed you will do today to prepare for that final day?

The Beginning of the Last Day and the Two Trumpet Blasts
Al-Sanusi mentions four opinions on the Last Day's beginning and clarifies the two trumpet blasts.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The Last Day begins with the second trumpet, which revives the dead.

After defining the Last Day, al-Sanusi turns to its beginning. He lists four opinions, then highlights the most prominent one—the second trumpet blast. This passage also explains the two trumpet blasts and their interval. The marginal commentary corrects the vocalization of 'sa'q'.
