The Heavenly Books: Definition and Reported Counts
Sanusi defines heavenly books and reports various enumerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars define heavenly books and the various reported numbers of revealed scriptures, including the four major books.

Core Idea
The heavenly books include scrolls and major scriptures, with differing counts reported.

This lesson introduces the concept of heavenly books (al-kutub al-samawiyya) as defined by Sanusi in his commentary. The passage presents multiple traditions about how many books were revealed, ranging from 104 to 114, with varying assignments to prophets. Understanding these different reports sets the stage for the correct belief, which will be clarified in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Understand what 'heavenly books' means: books sent down in tablets or via an angel, including scrolls (suhuf).
Note the two modes of revelation: in tablets or on the tongue of an angel.
2
Identify the different counts
Find the three main enumerations: 104 (Seth 60, Abraham 30, Moses 10 + 4 books), 104 (Seth 50, Idris 30, Abraham & Moses 20 + 4 books), and 114 (Seth 50, Idris 30, Abraham 20, dispute on 10).
The passage attributes the 114 count to al-Tata'i.
3
Recognize the four major books
Notice that the Torah, Psalms, Gospel, and Qur'an are consistently mentioned across all reports.
These are the books that require detailed knowledge in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْكُتُبِ السَّمَاوِيَّةِ) أي: المنزلة من السماء في الألواح، أو على لسان مَلَكِ، والمراد بها : ما يَشْمَلُ الصُّحُفَ، وقد اشتهر أنها مائة وأربعة: صُحُفُ شِيثَ ستون، وصُحُفُ إبراهيم ثلاثون، وصحف موسى قبل التوراة عشرة، والكتب الأربعةُ : التوراة لموسى والزَّبُورُ لداود، والإنجيل لعيسى، والفرقان لسيدنا محمد كذا نَقَلَ بعضُ شُرَّاح الأربعين عن الخطيب. وقيل: صُحُفُ شيت خمسون، وصحف إدريس ثلاثون، وصحف إبراهيم وموسى عشرون بالسوية، والكتب الأربعةُ وقيل: إنها مائة وأربعة عشر: صحف شيث خمسون، وصحف إدريس ثلاثون، وصحف إبراهيم عشرون واختلف في عشرة فقيل لآدم، وقيل: لموسى والكتب الأربعة. وهذا القولُ ما نَصَّ عليه التَّتَائِيُّ في "شرح الرسالة" حيث قال: فائدة: الكتبُ المُنْزَلَةُ من السماء مائة وأربعة عشر إلى آخر ما تقدم. هذا، والتحقيق: الإمساك عن حَصْرِها في عددٍ، فيجب اعتقاد أن الله أنزل كتباً من السماء على الإجمال، نعم الكتب الأربعةُ يجب معرفتها تفصيلاً. (قوله: فِي الْأَلْوَاحِ) كالتوراة. (وقوله : أو عَلَى لِسَانِ مَلَكِ) أي: جبريل كالقرآن.
His saying: 'And the heavenly books' – meaning: those sent down from heaven in tablets, or upon the tongue of an angel. What is intended by them is that which includes the scrolls (suhuf). It has become well-known that they are one hundred and four: the scrolls of Seth sixty, the scrolls of Abraham thirty, the scrolls of Moses before the Torah ten, and the four books: the Torah of Moses, the Psalms (al-Zabur) of David, the Gospel (al-Injil) of Jesus, and the Criterion (al-Furqan) of our master Muhammad – thus some of the commentators of the Forty transmitted from al-Khatib. And it is said: the scrolls of Seth fifty, the scrolls of Idris thirty, the scrolls of Abraham and Moses twenty equally, and the four books. And it is said: they are one hundred and fourteen: the scrolls of Seth fifty, the scrolls of Idris thirty, the scrolls of Abraham twenty, and there is disagreement on ten: it is said they were for Adam, and it is said for Moses, and the four books. This is the statement that al-Tata'i explicitly stated in his 'Sharh al-Risala' where he said: 'A benefit: the books sent down from heaven are one hundred and fourteen...' up to what has preceded. Now, the verified position is: to refrain from restricting them to a number. So one must believe in general that Allah sent down books from heaven. Yes, the four books must be known in detail. (His saying: 'in the tablets') like the Torah. (His saying: 'or upon the tongue of an angel') meaning: Gabriel, like the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'and the heavenly books' from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin. It defines the heavenly books as those sent down in tablets or via an angel, including scrolls (suhuf). It then presents various enumerations of the revealed scriptures: the famous count of 104 (60 to Seth, 30 to Abraham, 10 to Moses before Torah, and the four books), another of 114 (with differing assignments of 10 scrolls), and a third of 104 with different numbers. The passage notes that al-Tata'i explicitly stated the 114 count. The verified position (al-tahqiq) follows: one must refrain from restricting the number; rather, belief in the divine books is required in general (ijmal), while the four major books must be known in detail (tafsil). Two marginal clarifications are appended: 'in the tablets' refers to something like the Torah, and 'upon the tongue of an angel' refers to Gabriel, as with the Qur'an.

الصحف
التوراة
الزبور
الإنجيل
الفرقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 50

Key Takeaway

Correct Belief: General Faith and Detailed Knowledge of the Four Books
Sanusi states the correct belief and clarifies modes of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the sound Islamic position is to believe in all revealed books in general while specifically knowing the four major scriptures.

Core Idea
Belief in divine books is obligatory in general, but the four major books require detailed knowledge.

After presenting various reports about the number of heavenly books, Sanusi provides the verified stance: one should not fixate on a specific number but believe that God sent down books in general. However, the four major books—Torah, Psalms, Gospel, and Qur'an—must be known in detail. This lesson also clarifies two marginal points about how books were revealed.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the verified position
Read the statement that one must refrain from restricting the number and believe in general that Allah sent down books.
This is the balanced approach avoiding excess or negligence.
2
Distinguish general vs detailed
Note that the four books (Torah, Psalms, Gospel, Qur'an) require specific knowledge (tafsil), while others are believed in general (ijmal).
This is the key takeaway: we must know these four by name.
3
Understand the modes of revelation
See the two ways: 'in tablets' (like the Torah) and 'upon the tongue of an angel' (like the Qur'an via Gabriel).
These clarify how the books were received.

It has become well-known that they are one hundred and four: the scrolls of Seth sixty, the scrolls of Abraham thirty, the scrolls of Moses before the Torah ten, and the four books: the Torah of Moses, the Psalms (al-Zabur) of David, the Gospel (al-Injil) of Jesus, and the Criterion (al-Furqan) of our master Muhammad – thus some of the commentators of the Forty transmitted from al-Khatib. And it is said: the scrolls of Seth fifty, the scrolls of Idris thirty, the scrolls of Abraham and Moses twenty equally, and the four books. And it is said: they are one hundred and fourteen: the scrolls of Seth fifty, the scrolls of Idris thirty, the scrolls of Abraham twenty, and there is disagreement on ten: it is said they were for Adam, and it is said for Moses, and the four books. This is the statement that al-Tata'i explicitly stated in his 'Sharh al-Risala' where he said: 'A benefit: the books sent down from heaven are one hundred and fourteen...' up to what has preceded. Now, the verified position is: to refrain from restricting them to a number. So one must believe in general that Allah sent down books from heaven. Yes, the four books must be known in detail. (His saying: 'in the tablets') like the Torah. (His saying: 'or upon the tongue of an angel') meaning: Gabriel, like the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'and the heavenly books' from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin. It defines the heavenly books as those sent down in tablets or via an angel, including scrolls (suhuf). It then presents various enumerations of the revealed scriptures: the famous count of 104 (60 to Seth, 30 to Abraham, 10 to Moses before Torah, and the four books), another of 114 (with differing assignments of 10 scrolls), and a third of 104 with different numbers. The passage notes that al-Tata'i explicitly stated the 114 count. The verified position (al-tahqiq) follows: one must refrain from restricting the number; rather, belief in the divine books is required in general (ijmal), while the four major books must be known in detail (tafsil). Two marginal clarifications are appended: 'in the tablets' refers to something like the Torah, and 'upon the tongue of an angel' refers to Gabriel, as with the Qur'an.

Key Terms
الإجمال
التفصيل
الألواح
لسان ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must know the exact number of all revealed books.
Correction: The verified position is to refrain from restricting the number and believe in general; only the four books require detailed knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which books must be known in detail?
A) All revealed books
B) The scrolls of Seth and Abraham
C) The four books: Torah, Psalms, Gospel, Qur'an
D) Only the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The sound belief is to affirm all divine books generally and to know the four major books specifically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you have not studied them, what is stopping you from learning about them? Commit today to read at least one of them with translation and reflection.
?

Or have you become numb to the miracle of revelation? Ask yourself: do you truly treat the Qur'an as a conversation from your Lord, or just as a text to be recited?
?
The verified position is to refrain from restricting the number of books to a specific count. This teaches humility in the face of the unseen. The Last Day: Definition and Beginning
Sanusi defines the Last Day, explains its name, and gives opinions on its beginning and end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the Last Day, why it is called 'Last', and the scholarly differences about when it begins and ends.

Core Idea
The Last Day is defined but its timeline has scholarly nuance.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin introduces one of the essential eschatological beliefs: the Last Day. He defines it, explains why it is called 'Last', and then presents various scholarly views on when it begins and ends. As you read, notice how the discussion balances certainty of doctrine with room for difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and English translation once to get the overall meaning. Notice the structure: definition, naming, beginning, end.
The passage includes several 'it is said' (قيل) phrases indicating different opinions.
2
Understand the definition and naming
Identify that the Last Day is the Day of Resurrection. Two reasons are given for its name: it is the last of days, or because there is no night after it. The commentary notes these are essentially the same.
The name emphasizes finality and the end of the worldly cycle.
3
List the opinions on the beginning
Find the three opinions: from the second trumpet blast, from the gathering (hashr), or from death. Notice the passage does not choose one, recognizing scholarly disagreement.
The second trumpet is a key eschatological event.
4
Examine the opinions on the end
Two views: either the Last Day has no end, or it ends with the entry into Paradise and Hell. The passage presents both without resolution.
Eternal consequences are a central Islamic belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ الذي هو يوم القيامة، وإنما وُصِفَ بالآخر؛ لأنه آخِرُ الأيَّام. وقيل: لأنه لا ليل بعده. وأوَّلُه من النفخة الثانية. وقيل: من الحشر. وقيل: من الموت ولا نِهَايَةَ له. وقيل: ينتهي بدخول أهل الجنة الجنةَ، ودخول أهل النار النار. (قوله: وقيل : لِأَنَّهُ لَا لَيْلَ... إلخ) هو بمعنى ما قبله، فهما قول واحد.
His saying: 'And the Last Day' which is the Day of Resurrection. It is described as 'Last' because it is the last of the days. And it is said: because there is no night after it. Its beginning is from the second trumpet blast. And it is said: from the Gathering. And it is said: from death, and it has no end. And it is said: it ends with the entry of the people of Paradise into Paradise and the entry of the people of Hell into Hell. (His saying: 'And it is said: because there is no night... etc.') it is synonymous with what precedes it, so they are one statement.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines the Last Day (al-yawm al-akhir). It gives the reason for its name: because it is the last day, or because there is no night after it. It then discusses the beginning of that day: some say from the second trumpet, some from the gathering (hashr), some from death. And its end: some say it has no end, some say it ends when people enter Paradise or Hell. The commentary adds that the two statements about 'no night' are basically the same.

The Detailed Process of Resurrection
Al-Bajuri describes the physical process of resurrection after the second trumpet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed physical process of resurrection as described by Al-Bajuri, including the stages of rain, bodily growth, angelic involvement, and the insertion of spirits.

Core Idea
Resurrection is a physical process: rain, growth of bodies, and spirits re-entering like poison in a sting.

Al-Bajuri expands on the sequence of events after the second trumpet blast. This lesson walks through the stages of physical resurrection, from the initial rain to the final insertion of spirits into bodies. The passage includes a clarifying note about the nature of the rain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the vivid descriptions of rain, growth, and the trumpet.
2
Identify the stages of resurrection
List the stages in order: the rain, the growth of bodies, the command to angels, the trumpet, the exit and entry of spirits.
The process is sequential.
3
Understand the marginal note
The note clarifies that the rain water is not actual semen but water in its form. Also, it equates al-nashr with al-ba'th.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.
4
Connect to broader eschatology
Reflect on how this detailed account relates to the general belief in bodily resurrection (ba'th).
Both terms are synonymous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تُمْطِرُ السماءُ ماءً كَمَنِي الرجال أربعين يوماً بشدَّةٍ كأفواه القُرَبِ، حتى يكون الماء من فوق الناس قَدْرَ اثني عَشَرَ ذراعاً، ثم يَأْمُرُ الله الأجساد، فتَنْبُتُ كنبات البَقْل، حتى إذا تكامَلَتْ، فكانت كما كانت يقول الله تعالى ليَحْيَى جبريل ومكائيل وإسرافيل، ثم يأمر إسرافيل، فيأخذ الصور - وهو قَرْن من نورٍ كهيئة البُوقِ الذي يُؤْمَرُ به، لكنه عظيمٌ كعَرْضِ السماء والأرض كما في الحديث ، ثم يدعو الله الأرواح، ويُلْقِيها في الصُّور، ويأمرُ إسرافيل بالنفخ، فتخرجُ الأرواحُ مثل النخل، فتمشي في الأجساد مَشْيَ السُّمّ في اللديغ، وذلك هو المسمى بـ: النَّشْرِ. (قوله: مَاءً كَمَنِيَ الرِّجَالِ منه يعلم أنه ليس منياً حقيقة، بل هو ماء على صورة مني. (قوله: وَذَلِكَ) أي: إدخال الأرواح في الأجساد، فالنشر والبعث بمعنى واحد، وهو إحياء الأبدان وإدخال الأرواح فيها.
The sky will rain water like male seminal fluid for forty days with intensity like the mouths of waterskins, until the water is above the people to the extent of twelve cubits. Then Allah will command the bodies, and they will sprout like the growth of vegetation. When they are complete, they become as they were. Then Allah will say to the living (yuhyi) Gabriel, Michael, and Israfil. Then He commands Israfil, and he takes the trumpet (al-sur) – which is a horn of light in the form of a trumpet that is blown, but it is great, the width of the heavens and the earth as in the hadith. Then Allah calls the spirits, and throws them into the trumpet, and orders Israfil to blow. So the spirits come out like date palms, and they walk into the bodies like the walking of poison in a stung person. That is what is called 'al-Nashr' (the resurrection). (His saying: 'water like male seminal fluid' – from it it is known that it is not actually semen, but water in the form of semen. (His saying: 'and that' – meaning the insertion of spirits into bodies. So al-Nashr and al-Ba'th are synonymous, and it is the reviving of bodies and the insertion of spirits into them.)

Commentary
This passage describes the physical process of resurrection (al-nashr) after the second trumpet. It details the rain that falls, the growth of bodies from the earth, the role of the three prominent angels, the trumpet, the emergence and insertion of spirits. The marginal note clarifies that the water is not literal semen, and that nashr is synonymous with ba'th (revival).

Forms of People on the Day of Gathering
Al-Bajuri defines the Gathering and enumerates eight categories of people with their corresponding sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that on the Day of Gathering, people are assembled in various forms that correspond to their worldly sins.

Core Idea
Your deeds shape your appearance in the afterlife.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining the Gathering (al-Hashr) as the driving of people to the place of assembly. He then quotes al-Tha'labi to describe the different states people will be in, each form reflecting a specific sin committed in this world. This passage reminds us that our actions have visible consequences in the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening definition of al-Hashr. It means the driving of people to the plain of assembly.
2
Identify the first three forms
Notice the first three forms: riders, walkers on feet, and those walking on their faces. These represent different degrees of righteousness and sin.
3
Match each form to its corresponding sin
For each subsequent form (apes, pigs, blind, deaf and dumb, etc.), identify the sin mentioned. This shows a direct link between worldly behavior and afterlife appearance.
4
Reflect on the list
Consider the completeness of the list: it includes fornicators, those who consume unlawful earnings, unjust judges, proud scholars, hypocritical preachers, those who harm neighbours, and those crucified. What do these have in common?
5
Draw a lesson
Ask yourself: How does this enumeration encourage you to avoid these sins?

As for the Gathering (al-Hashr): it is the driving of people to the place of gathering. And it is transmitted from al-Tha'labi that people in the Gathering are of various degrees: among them is the rider, among them is the walker on his feet, among them is the one who walks on his face, among them is the one who is in the form of apes – they are the fornicators; among them is the one who is in the form of pigs – they are those who used to consume unlawful earnings (al-suht) and taxes (al-maks); among them is the blind – he is the unjust judge; among them is the deaf and dumb – he is the one who is amazed by his own knowledge; among them is the one who bites his tongue and pus flows from his mouth – they are the preachers whose actions contradict their words; among them is the one whose hands and legs are cut off – they are those who harm neighbours; among them is the one who is crucified on trunks.

The Process of Resurrection (al-Nashr) and Its Clarification
Sanusi describes the resurrection process; the hashya clarifies the nature of the rain and terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key events of the resurrection (al-nashr) as described by Sanusi, including the symbolic rain, the sprouting of bodies, and the role of the Trumpet, along with the clarification that al-nashr and al-ba'th are synonymous.

Core Idea
Resurrection is the reviving of bodies by inserting spirits, initiated by a special rain and the blowing of the Trumpet.

Sanusi presents a vivid narrative of the resurrection, describing how bodies sprout from a rain like semen and spirits enter them like poison. The hashya clarifies that this rain is not literal semen and that al-nashr is the same as al-ba'th. This lesson guides you through this important process of the sam'iyyat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to absorb the narrative sequence. Notice the vivid imagery used.
The hashya comments are embedded in parentheses.
2
Identify the sequence of events
List the steps: the rain, bodies sprouting, Allah's command to the angels, Israfil taking the Trumpet, and spirits entering the bodies.
This sequence forms the process of al-nashr.
3
Understand the clarifications
The hashya clarifies two points: the rain is water in the form of semen, not actual semen; and 'that' refers to inserting spirits, making nashr and ba'th synonymous.
This resolves potential misunderstandings.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this description emphasizes Allah's absolute power to recreate life, a core belief in Islamic eschatology.
The details reinforce the reality of the resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تُمْطِرُ السماءُ ماءً كَمَنِي الرجال أربعين يوماً بشدَّةٍ كأفواه القُرَبِ، حتى يكون الماء من فوق الناس قَدْرَ اثني عَشَرَ ذراعاً، ثم يَأْمُرُ الله الأجساد، فتَنْبُتُ كنبات البَقْل، حتى إذا تكامَلَتْ، فكانت كما كانت يقول الله تعالى ليَحْيَى جبريل ومكائيل وإسرافيل، ثم يأمر إسرافيل، فيأخذ الصور - وهو قَرْن من نورٍ كهيئة البُوقِ الذي يُؤْمَرُ به، لكنه عظيمٌ كعَرْضِ السماء والأرض كما في الحديث، ثم يدعو الله الأرواح، ويُلْقِيها في الصُّور، ويأمرُ إسرافيل بالنفخ، فتخرجُ الأرواحُ مثل النخل، فتمشي في الأجساد مَشْيَ السُّمّ في اللديغ، وذلك هو المسمى بـ: النَّشْرِ.
(قوله: مَاءً كَمَنِيَ الرِّجَالِ منه يعلم أنه ليس منياً حقيقة، بل هو ماء على صورة مني.)
(قوله: وَذَلِكَ) أي: إدخال الأرواح في الأجساد، فالنشر والبعث بمعنى واحد، وهو إحياء الأبدان وإدخال الأرواح فيها.
The sky will rain water like the semen of men for forty days with intensity like the mouths of waterskins, until the water above people is twelve cubits [high]. Then Allah commands the bodies, and they sprout like the growth of herbs, until when they are completed, they become as they were. Allah says to Jibril, Mika'il, and Israfil: 'Let them live.' Then He commands Israfil, and he takes the Trumpet (Sur) – which is a horn of light in the form of the horn that is blown, but it is great like the expanse of the sky and the earth as in the hadith. Then Allah calls the spirits and casts them into the Trumpet, and commands Israfil to blow, and the spirits come out like palm trees, and they walk into the bodies like the walk of poison into the one stung. That is what is called 'al-Nashr'.
(His saying: 'water like the semen of men' – from it is known that it is not actual semen, but water in the form of semen.)
(His saying: 'and that' means: the insertion of spirits into bodies. So al-nashr and al-ba'th are synonymous, meaning the reviving of bodies and the insertion of spirits into them.)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines and describes the resurrection (al-nashr). The main text by Sanusi presents a vivid narrative: a rain like semen falls for forty days, causing bodies to sprout like plants. Then Allah commands the angels Jibril, Mika'il, and Israfil to revive them. Israfil takes the Trumpet (Sur), described as a great horn of light, and after Allah casts the spirits into it, Israfil blows and the spirits emerge like palm trees and enter the bodies like poison spreading. This process is named al-nashr. The hashya commentary clarifies two points: first, the rain is not literal semen but water resembling it; second, the phrase 'and that' refers to the insertion of spirits into bodies, confirming that nashr and ba'th (resurrection) are the same – the reviving of bodies with spirits.

Key Terms
النشر (al-nashr)
البعث (al-ba'th)
الصور (al-Sur)
الأرواح (al-arwah)
الأجساد (al-ajsad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rain that falls is actual semen.
Correction: The hashya clarifies it is water in the form of semen, not literal semen.

Checkpoint

A) They are opposites.
B) They are synonymous, both referring to the reviving of bodies and insertion of spirits.
C) Al-nashr is the blowing of the Trumpet, while al-ba'th is the gathering.
D) Al-nashr is the rain, and al-ba'th is the sprouting of bodies.

Key Takeaway
Al-nashr is the reviving of bodies through the insertion of spirits after the blowing of the Trumpet, and it is synonymous with al-ba'th.

?

The Gathering (al-Hashr) and the Diverse States of People
Sanusi defines al-hashr and enumerates the different forms of people from al-Tha'labi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Hashr and the various forms people will appear in on the Day of Judgment based on their sins, as narrated from al-Tha'labi.

Core Idea
On the Day of Gathering, sins manifest in visible forms.

This lesson introduces the concept of al-Hashr (the Gathering) and presents a vivid description from al-Tha'labi of how different types of sinners will appear in the afterlife. The passage shows that each sin has a specific corresponding form, illustrating the principle that actions are recompensed in kind.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-Hashr
Start by reading the opening sentence that defines al-Hashr as the driving of people to the place of assembly. Notice the simplicity of the definition before the detailed enumeration.
The word 'al-mahshar' is the place of gathering.
2
Identify the categories of people
The passage lists several categories of people based on their sins. As you read, note the pattern: 'Among them are... they are...' Pay attention to the variety of forms, from riding to walking on faces.
Some forms are animal-like, others are physical impairments or punishments.
3
Connect each sin to its form
For each category, link the sin mentioned to the form given. Ask yourself why that particular form fits the sin. This helps in understanding the justice of recompense.
For example, fornicators become monkeys—perhaps reflecting animalistic behavior.
4
Reflect on the moral message
Consider the overall lesson: every sin has a consequence that is fitting. This should inspire mindfulness in actions and avoidance of the listed sins.
The forms are literal descriptions in the hereafter according to the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحشر : فهو سَوْقُ الناس إلى المحشر ونُقِلَ عن الثَّعْلَبِي: أن الناس في الحشر متفاوتَةٌ، فمنهم الراكِبُ، ومنهم الماشي على رجليه ومنهم من يمشي على وجهه، ومنهم من هو على صورة القِرَدَةِ، وهم الزَّنَاةُ، ومنهم من هو على صورة الخنازير، وهم الذين كانوا يأكلون السُّحْتَ والمكس، ومنهم الأعمى، وهو الجائر في الحكم، ومنهم الأصمُّ الأبكم، وهو من يُعْجَبُ بعلمه، ومنهم من يَمْضَعُ لسانه، ويسيلُ القَيْحُ من فمه، وهم الوُهَّاظُ الذين تخالِفُ أعمالهم أقوالهم. ومنهم من هو مقطوع الأيدي والأرجل، وهم الذين يُؤذُون الجيران، ومنهم من يُصْلَبُ على جذوع من النار، وهم الشعاة بالناس إلى السلطان، ومنهم من هو أشدُّ نَتَناً من الجِيفَةِ، وهم الذي يُقْبلُون على اللذات والشهوات، ويمنعون حق الله من أموالهم، ومنهم من يَلْبَسُ جُبَّةً سابغة من قَطِرَانٍ، وهم أهل الكبر والعُجب والخيلاء.
As for al-Hashr: it is the driving of people to the place of assembly (al-mahshar). It is transmitted from al-Tha'labi: verily people in the gathering are diverse. Among them are riders, among them are walkers on their feet, and among them are those who walk on their faces. Among them are those in the form of monkeys – they are the fornicators. Among them are those in the form of pigs – they are those who used to consume illicit earnings (suhṭ) and taxation (maks). Among them are the blind – they are the unjust in judgement. Among them are the deaf and dumb – they are those who are amazed by their own knowledge. Among them are those who chew their tongues and pus flows from their mouths – they are the preachers whose actions contradict their words. Among them are those with cut hands and feet – they are those who harm neighbors. Among them are those crucified on trunks of fire – they are those who slander people to the ruler. Among them are those more foul-smelling than a corpse – they are those who indulge in pleasures and desires and withhold Allah's right from their wealth. Among them are those who wear a long cloak of tar – they are the people of pride, self-admiration, and arrogance.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines al-Hashr (the Gathering) and describes the various forms people will appear in on the Day of Judgment, based on a report from al-Tha'labi. The definition states that hashr is the act of driving people to the place of assembly (al-mahshar). Then a list of categories is given, each linking a specific sin to a corresponding physical or imagined form in the afterlife. For example, fornicators appear as monkeys, those who consume illicit earnings or bribes appear as pigs, unjust judges are blind, proud scholars are deaf and dumb, hypocritical preachers chew their tongues, harmful neighbors have amputated limbs, slanderers are crucified, hedonists stink like corpses, and the arrogant wear garments of tar. This illustrates the belief that sins manifest visibly in the resurrection.

Key Terms
الحشر (al-hashr)
المحشر (al-mahshar)
السحت (al-suhṭ)
المكس (al-maks)
الثعلبي (al-Tha'labi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The forms listed are merely metaphorical or symbolic.
Correction: The passage presents these as real, literal states in the hereafter. The forms are direct consequences of the sins committed in this world.

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Tha'labi, who will appear in the form of monkeys?
A) Those who harm neighbors
B) The proud and arrogant
C) The fornicators
D) The unjust judges

Key Takeaway
The forms in the Gathering are direct and fitting consequences of one's deeds in this life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to be transformed into one of the forms described in this lesson based on your current state, which one would it be, and what specific sin would you need to abandon starting today to avoid that fate?
?
The one amazed by his own knowledge becomes deaf and dumb. When you learn something new, do you feel pride or gratitude? What concrete step can you take today to cultivate humility in seeking knowledge?
?
The preachers whose actions contradict their words are depicted in a horrifying state. Is there any area of your life where your actions do not match what you say you believe? What is the first action you will change this week to realign them?

The Great Intercession and the Seventy Thousand
Sanusi narrates the events at the mahshar: intercession and exception of seventy thousand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the events at the mahshar, including the Great Intercession of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the exception of seventy thousand who enter Paradise without reckoning.

Core Idea
The Prophet's intercession for judgment begins a cascade of mercy, including seventy thousand entering Paradise without reckoning.

Sanusi presents a vivid description of the terrors of the Day of Gathering (al-mahshar) and how the prophets each excuse themselves from interceding until the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) accepts the role. This passage also mentions the hadith about seventy thousand entering Paradise without reckoning, and the Prophet's request for increase. Together, these events highlight the Prophet's unique status and the boundless mercy of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow before you interpret.
Notice the dramatic progression from terror to intercession.
2
Identify the stages of the intercession
Trace the sequence: people arrive at mahshar, terror, seeking prophets, excuses, Prophet's acceptance, prostration, intercession for judgment.
3
Understand the exclusivity of the Great Intercession
The text says 'This is the Great Intercession, exclusive to him.' Reflect on why it is exclusive and what it accomplishes.
Compare with other intercessions that he opens for others.
4
Examine the exception of the seventy thousand
Read the hadith about the seventy thousand and the increase. Note that they enter without reckoning, and the Prophet asked for more.
Consider the significance of this mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عند وصولهم إلى المحشر يَقِفُون فيه، وتصطف الملائكة محدّقين حولهم، وتدنو الشمس من رؤوسهم حتى ما يكون بينها وبينهم إلا قَدْرُ ميل - أي: میل المُكْحُلَةِ لا الميل المعروف ، فحينئذٍ يَشْتَدُّ الخوفُ والهَوْلُ، ويَعْظُمُ الكربُ، فيتمنون الانصراف ولو إلى النار، ثم بعد طول الموقف عليهم يُلْهَمُون أن الأنبياء هم الواسطة بين الله وبين خلقه، فيذهبون إليهم يستشفعون بهم واحداً بعد واحد، فيتنصل - أي: يعتذر - كلّ منهم بما وَقَعَ لَهُ من صورة الخطيئة، ويقول: لستُ لها، لستُ لها، نفسي نفسي. فإذا انتهى الأمرُ للرئيس الأعظم والسيد الأكمل الأفخم قال: أنا لها، أمَّتي أمتي، ثم يَخِرُّ ساجداً تحت العَرْشِ كسجود الصلاة، فيقال: يا محمد، ارفع رأسك، وسَلْ تُعْطَ، واشفعْ تُشَفَّعْ، فيرفع رأسَهُ، ويشفَعُ في فصل القضاء. وهذه هي الشفاعةُ مختصة به ، وله شفاعاتٌ أَخَرُ ، بل ولغيره من باقي الأنبياء والعلماء والصالحين؛ لأنهم يتجاسرون على ذلك بسبب شفاعته ، فهو الذي يفتح لهم باب الشفاعة. ثم بعد ذلك يحاسَبُون، إلا من وَرَدَ الحديث باستثنائه، فإنه وَرَدَ أنه قال: يَدْخُلُ الْجَنَّةَ مِنْ أُمَّتِي سَبْعُونَ أَلْفاً بِغَيْرِ حِسَابٍ»، فقيل له: هلا استزدَتَّ ربَّك؟ فقال: «اسْتَرَدْتُهُ فَزَادَنِي مَعْ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ سَبْعِينَ أَلْفاً، فقيل له: هلا استزدت ربك؟ (قوله: بِمَا وَقَعَ لَهُ مِنْ صُورَةِ الْخَطِيئَةِ كقول سيدنا نوح : رَبِّ لَا نَذَرْ عَلَى الْأَرْضِ مِنَ الْكَفِرِينَ دَيَّارًا)
Then when they arrive at the place of assembly (al-mahshar), they stand there, and the angels line up surrounding them. The sun draws near their heads until there is only a mile between it and them – i.e., the mile of the kohl applicator, not the known mile. Then fear and terror intensify, and distress becomes great, so they wish to depart even to the Fire. Then after the long standing, they are inspired that the prophets are the intermediaries between Allah and His creation. So they go to them seeking their intercession one after another. Each of them (the prophets) excuses himself due to what occurred from him of the form of sin, saying: 'Not me, not me, myself, myself.' When the matter reaches the Greatest Leader, the most perfect and most magnificent Master, he says: 'I am for it, my community, my community.' Then he falls prostrate under the Throne like the prostration of prayer. It is said: 'O Muhammad, raise your head, ask and you shall be given, intercede and your intercession shall be accepted.' So he raises his head and intercedes for the judgment to commence. This is the Great Intercession, and it is exclusive to him. He has other intercessions, and indeed others among the remaining prophets, scholars, and righteous have intercession as well – because they dare to do so due to his intercession, for he opens the door of intercession for them. Then after that, they are called to account, except those whom the hadith exempts. For it is reported that he said: 'Seventy thousand of my community will enter Paradise without reckoning.' It was said to him: 'Why did you not ask your Lord for more?' He said: 'I asked Him, and He increased for me with each one seventy thousand.' It was said to him: 'Why did you not ask your Lord for more?' (His saying: 'due to what occurred from him of the form of sin' – like the saying of our master Nuh: 'Lord, leave not upon the earth any inhabitant of the disbelievers.')
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage narrates the events after the gathering at the mahshar. It begins with the description of the assembly: the angels surround the people, the sun approaches extremely close (a metaphorical mile), and the terror is overwhelming. The people then seek intercession from the prophets one by one, but each prophet excuses himself due to a prior sin (e.g., Nuh's prayer against the disbelievers). Finally, they come to the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), who accepts the intercession, prostrates, and is commanded to intercede for the judgment to begin. This is the 'Great Intercession' (al-shafa'ah al-uzma), which is exclusive to him. He also opens the door for other prophets, scholars, and righteous to intercede. After this, people are brought to account, except for the seventy thousand mentioned in the hadith who enter Paradise without reckoning. The Prophet requested more, and each of those seventy thousand brings another seventy thousand. The hashya gives an example of a prophet's excuse: Nuh's statement 'Lord, leave not upon the earth any inhabitant of the disbelievers.'

Key Terms
الشفاعة العظمى (al-shafa'ah al-uzma)
فصل القضاء (fasl al-qada')
المحشر (al-mahshar)
حساب (hisab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Great Intercession is to bring sinners into Paradise directly.
Correction: The Great Intercession as described is for the commencement of judgment (fasl al-qada'), not for entry into Paradise. It is the first step that allows other intercessions.

Checkpoint
What is the 'Great Intercession' (al-shafa'ah al-uzma)?
A) The intercession for sinners to enter Paradise
B) The intercession for the judgment to begin (fasl al-qada'), exclusive to Prophet Muhammad
C) The intercession of all prophets collectively
D) The intercession for entering Paradise without reckoning

Key Takeaway
The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) is uniquely granted the Great Intercession for the judgment to begin, and through his intercession, seventy thousand of his community, multiplied, enter Paradise without reckoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine standing on that day, with the sun nearly upon you, and you seek intercession from every prophet, each one saying 'myself, myself,' would your own deeds give you any confidence? What in your life today would make you think you might be among the seventy thousand who enter without reckoning? Write down one specific change you will make this week to increase your hope of being among them.
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'my community' and prostrated for the judgment to begin. Are you truly part of that community in your daily actions? What evidence do you have that you are following him not just in ritual but in character? What is one character trait you need to change to be a more loyal follower?
?
The Prophet asked for more, and Allah gave him with each one seventy thousand. Do you rely on his intercession enough? What is one du'a you can make tonight asking Allah to include you in that number, and what specific action will you take tomorrow to live as one who hopes for that mercy?

Who Is Weighed on the Day of Judgment?
Sanusi enumerates exceptions to the weighing of deeds and clarifies the ruling for disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which individuals are exempted from the weighing of deeds and the correct ruling on weighing the deeds of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Deeds are weighed for most, but prophets, angels, and those entering Paradise without reckoning are exempt.

Sanusi begins by stating the general principle that deeds are weighed, then immediately clarifies exceptions. He also addresses the disputed issue of whether disbelievers' deeds are weighed, settling it by affirming they are, and explains a Quranic verse that might suggest otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the exceptions
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the categories of people who are exempt from the weighing of deeds.
The exceptions are prophets, angels, and all those who enter Paradise without reckoning.
2
Understand the two opinions on disbelievers
Notice that there are two opinions regarding the weighing of disbelievers' deeds. Identify which one Sanusi considers the most correct.
The most correct opinion is that their deeds are weighed.
3
See how the Quranic verse is reconciled
Sanusi interprets the verse 'We shall not establish for them any weight' by explaining that it means 'no beneficial weight'.
This verse does not contradict the weighing of disbelievers' deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تُوزَنُ أعمالهم إلا من وَرَدَ النصُّ باستثنائهم كالأنبياء والملائكة وسائرِ مَنْ يدخُلُ الجنة بغير حساب، وفي وَزْنِ أعمال الكفار قولان، والأصح أنها تُوزَنُ، وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا نُقِيمُ لَهُمْ يَوْمَ الْقِيِّمَةِ وَزْنا ) (۲) فعلى حَذْفِ الوصف، أي: وزناً نافعاً، وجمهورُ المفسّرين على أن الموزون: الكتب التي هي صحائف الأعمال. وقيل: نَفْسُ الأعمال. وقيل: تُصَوَّرُ الأعمال الصالحة بصورة نورانيةٍ حَسَنَةٍ، وتُطْرَحُ في كَفَّة النور، فتنقُلُ بفضل الله تعالى، وتُصَوَّرُ الأعمال السيئة بصورة ظلمانية قبيحة، وتُطْرَحُ في كَفَّة الظُّلْمَة، فَتَخِفُّ بعدل الله تعالى. وقال بعضهم: إن الله يخلق أجساماً بعدد الأعمال كما جاء الأثر أيضاً، وظاهرُ كلام العلماء المأخوذ من الآثار: أن خِفَّةَ الميزان وثِقَلَهُ على كيفيته المعهودة في الدنيا: ما ثَقُلَ نَزَلَ إلى أسفل، ثم يُرْفَعُ إلى عليين، وما خَفَّ طَاشَ إلى أعلى، ثم يَنْزِلُ إِلى سِجِّين، وبذلك صَرَّحَ القُرْبِيُّ. وقال بعض المتأخرين: عَمَلُ المؤمن إذا رَجَحَ صَعَدَ وتسفّلَتْ سيئاته، وأما الكافرُ؛ فَتَسْفُلُ كَفَّتُه لخلق الأخرى من الحسنات. والأصح أن الميزانَ واحدٌ لا تعدُّدَ فيه. وقيل: لكلّ أمةٍ ميزان وقيل: لكلّ مكلَّف ميزان. وقيل: للمؤمن موازين بعدد خيراته وأنواع حسناته، فلصلاته ميزان، ولصومه ميزانٌ، وهَلُمَّ جرّاً. ولا يَرِدُ على الأول قوله تعالى: وَنَضَعُ الْمَوَزِينَ الْقِسْطَ ) (۱) لأن جَمْعَهُ في ذلك للتعظيم والذي يَزِنُ به جبريلُ، فيأخذ بعموده ويَنْظُرُ إلى لسانه، وميكائيل أمين عليه.
Then their deeds are weighed except for those whom the text (al-nass) has exempted, such as the prophets, the angels, and all those who enter Paradise without reckoning. Concerning the weighing of the deeds of the disbelievers, there are two opinions, and the most correct is that they are weighed. As for His statement, Exalted is He: “We shall not establish for them on the Day of Resurrection any weight” (Qur’an 18:105), it is interpreted by deleting the attribute, i.e., any beneficial weight. The majority of the exegetes (mufassirun) hold that what is weighed are the books which are the scrolls of deeds. And it is said: the deeds themselves. And it is said: the righteous deeds are represented in a luminous, beautiful form, and are placed in the pan of light, and they become heavy by the favor of Allah, Exalted is He; and the evil deeds are represented in a dark, ugly form, and are placed in the pan of darkness, and they become light by the justice of Allah, Exalted is He. And some said: Allah creates bodies according to the number of deeds, as the tradition (athar) also came. And the apparent meaning of the scholars’ words taken from the traditions is that the lightness and heaviness of the balance are according to its customary manner in this world: whatever is heavy descends downwards, then is raised to ‘Illiyin, and whatever is light flies upwards, then descends to Sijjin. And al-Qurbi stated this explicitly. And some later scholars said: The deed of the believer, when it preponderates, ascends, and his evil deeds sink; but as for the disbeliever, his pan sinks because of the other pan’s lack of good deeds. And the most correct is that the Balance (mizan) is one, with no multiplicity in it. And it is said: For every nation there is a balance; and it is said: For every responsible person (mukallaf) there is a balance. And it is said: For the believer there are balances equal to the number of his good deeds and the types of his good acts; so for his prayer there is a balance, for his fasting a balance, and so on. And the first opinion is not refuted by His statement, Exalted is He: “And We shall place the just balances” (Qur’an 21:47) because its plural is for glorification. And the one who weighs with it is Gabriel; he takes hold of its pillar and looks at its tongue, and Michael is entrusted with it.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition by Sanusi on the weighing of deeds (mizan). It begins by stating that deeds are weighed, with exceptions for certain individuals (prophets, angels, those entering Paradise without reckoning). Then it addresses the weighing of disbelievers’ deeds (the correct view is that they are weighed). It explains the Qur’anic verse about no weight being established for disbelievers by stating it means no beneficial weight. Then it presents three opinions on what is weighed: the scrolls of deeds, the deeds themselves, or the deeds represented as forms (luminous for good, dark for evil). It then adds additional opinions: some say Allah creates bodies corresponding to deeds; the apparent understanding from traditions is that the balance works like a physical balance (heavy descends, light ascends); later scholars said the believer’s good deed makes his pan ascend and bad deeds sink, while the disbeliever’s pan sinks due to lack of good deeds. Then it states the most correct view: the Balance is one, not multiple, and gives alternative opinions (for each nation, for each mukallaf, or multiple balances for each believer’s good deeds). It addresses a potential objection from the plural 'balances' in the Qur’an, explaining it is for glorification. Finally, it mentions that Gabriel is the one who weighs, standing with a pillar and tongue, and Michael is entrusted with it. This passage clarifies the eschatological reality of the mizan, distinguishing between the different views held in the tradition, and affirms the unity of the balance while acknowledging other possibilities.

Key Terms
وزن الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disbelievers' deeds are not weighed at all because of the verse 'We shall not establish for them any weight'.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the verse means no beneficial weight; their deeds are weighed but without benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the most correct opinion regarding the weighing of disbelievers' deeds?
A) They are not weighed.
B) They are weighed.
C) Only their good deeds are weighed.
D) Only their evil deeds are weighed.

Key Takeaway
The weighing of deeds is universal except for specific individuals, and disbelievers' deeds are weighed but hold no beneficial weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the Prophet ﷺ and the angels are exempt from weighing, but you are not. Is there a hidden sin or habit in your life that you consider 'too small' to matter? How will you uproot it starting today, knowing it will be weighed?
?
Disbelievers' deeds have no beneficial weight because of their lack of faith. Think of a good deed you do regularly: is your intention pure for Allah alone, or is there a trace of showing off? What can you do to purify your intention by your next prayer?
?
The verse 'We shall not establish for them any weight' is interpreted to mean no beneficial weight. What is one deed you are counting on that may be 'weightless' due to a flaw in your faith or sincerity? Commit to one concrete step to restore its weight.

The Nature and Number of the Balance
Sanusi presents opinions on what is weighed and the number of balances, concluding with the angels involved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main scholarly opinions on what is actually weighed (scrolls, deeds, or representations) and the correct view on the number of balances.

Core Idea
The balance is one, but there are multiple interpretations of what is weighed.

Sanusi delves into the details of the weighing process, presenting various views from the scholars about what exactly is placed on the balance. He then addresses whether there is one balance or many, and concludes with the most correct view that the Balance is one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions on what is weighed
Read the passage and list the three opinions: scrolls of deeds, deeds themselves, and deeds represented as luminous or dark forms.
The majority hold that scrolls are weighed.
2
Consider the additional opinions on the process
Note the opinion that bodies are created for deeds, and the description of the balance's physics (heavy descends, light ascends).
These are based on traditions and physical analogy.
3
Learn the debate on the number of balances
Sanusi presents four opinions: one balance for all, one per nation, one per responsible person, or multiple balances per believer. He declares the first as most correct.
The plural 'balances' in the Qur’an is for glorification, not multiplicity.
4
Note the roles of Gabriel and Michael
The passage ends by stating that Gabriel weighs with the balance, and Michael is entrusted with it.
This adds an angelic dimension to the process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تُوزَنُ أعمالهم إلا من وَرَدَ النصُّ باستثنائهم كالأنبياء والملائكة وسائرِ مَنْ يدخُلُ الجنة بغير حساب، وفي وَزْنِ أعمال الكفار قولان، والأصح أنها تُوزَنُ، وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا نُقِيمُ لَهُمْ يَوْمَ الْقِيِّمَةِ وَزْنا ) (۲) فعلى حَذْفِ الوصف، أي: وزناً نافعاً، وجمهورُ المفسّرين على أن الموزون: الكتب التي هي صحائف الأعمال. وقيل: نَفْسُ الأعمال. وقيل: تُصَوَّرُ الأعمال الصالحة بصورة نورانيةٍ حَسَنَةٍ، وتُطْرَحُ في كَفَّة النور، فتنقُلُ بفضل الله تعالى، وتُصَوَّرُ الأعمال السيئة بصورة ظلمانية قبيحة، وتُطْرَحُ في كَفَّة الظُّلْمَة، فَتَخِفُّ بعدل الله تعالى. وقال بعضهم: إن الله يخلق أجساماً بعدد الأعمال كما جاء الأثر أيضاً، وظاهرُ كلام العلماء المأخوذ من الآثار: أن خِفَّةَ الميزان وثِقَلَهُ على كيفيته المعهودة في الدنيا: ما ثَقُلَ نَزَلَ إلى أسفل، ثم يُرْفَعُ إلى عليين، وما خَفَّ طَاشَ إلى أعلى، ثم يَنْزِلُ إِلى سِجِّين، وبذلك صَرَّحَ القُرْبِيُّ. وقال بعض المتأخرين: عَمَلُ المؤمن إذا رَجَحَ صَعَدَ وتسفّلَتْ سيئاته، وأما الكافرُ؛ فَتَسْفُلُ كَفَّتُه لخلق الأخرى من الحسنات. والأصح أن الميزانَ واحدٌ لا تعدُّدَ فيه. وقيل: لكلّ أمةٍ ميزان وقيل: لكلّ مكلَّف ميزان. وقيل: للمؤمن موازين بعدد خيراته وأنواع حسناته، فلصلاته ميزان، ولصومه ميزانٌ، وهَلُمَّ جرّاً. ولا يَرِدُ على الأول قوله تعالى: وَنَضَعُ الْمَوَزِينَ الْقِسْطَ ) (۱) لأن جَمْعَهُ في ذلك للتعظيم والذي يَزِنُ به جبريلُ، فيأخذ بعموده ويَنْظُرُ إلى لسانه، وميكائيل أمين عليه.
Then their deeds are weighed except for those whom the text (al-nass) has exempted, such as the prophets, the angels, and all those who enter Paradise without reckoning. Concerning the weighing of the deeds of the disbelievers, there are two opinions, and the most correct is that they are weighed. As for His statement, Exalted is He: “We shall not establish for them on the Day of Resurrection any weight” (Qur’an 18:105), it is interpreted by deleting the attribute, i.e., any beneficial weight. The majority of the exegetes (mufassirun) hold that what is weighed are the books which are the scrolls of deeds. And it is said: the deeds themselves. And it is said: the righteous deeds are represented in a luminous, beautiful form, and are placed in the pan of light, and they become heavy by the favor of Allah, Exalted is He; and the evil deeds are represented in a dark, ugly form, and are placed in the pan of darkness, and they become light by the justice of Allah, Exalted is He. And some said: Allah creates bodies according to the number of deeds, as the tradition (athar) also came. And the apparent meaning of the scholars’ words taken from the traditions is that the lightness and heaviness of the balance are according to its customary manner in this world: whatever is heavy descends downwards, then is raised to ‘Illiyin, and whatever is light flies upwards, then descends to Sijjin. And al-Qurbi stated this explicitly. And some later scholars said: The deed of the believer, when it preponderates, ascends, and his evil deeds sink; but as for the disbeliever, his pan sinks because of the other pan’s lack of good deeds. And the most correct is that the Balance (mizan) is one, with no multiplicity in it. And it is said: For every nation there is a balance; and it is said: For every responsible person (mukallaf) there is a balance. And it is said: For the believer there are balances equal to the number of his good deeds and the types of his good acts; so for his prayer there is a balance, for his fasting a balance, and so on. And the first opinion is not refuted by His statement, Exalted is He: “And We shall place the just balances” (Qur’an 21:47) because its plural is for glorification. And the one who weighs with it is Gabriel; he takes hold of its pillar and looks at its tongue, and Michael is entrusted with it.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition by Sanusi on the weighing of deeds (mizan). It begins by stating that deeds are weighed, with exceptions for certain individuals (prophets, angels, those entering Paradise without reckoning). Then it addresses the weighing of disbelievers’ deeds (the correct view is that they are weighed). It explains the Qur’anic verse about no weight being established for disbelievers by stating it means no beneficial weight. Then it presents three opinions on what is weighed: the scrolls of deeds, the deeds themselves, or the deeds represented as forms (luminous for good, dark for evil). It then adds additional opinions: some say Allah creates bodies corresponding to deeds; the apparent understanding from traditions is that the balance works like a physical balance (heavy descends, light ascends); later scholars said the believer’s good deed makes his pan ascend and bad deeds sink, while the disbeliever’s pan sinks due to lack of good deeds. Then it states the most correct view: the Balance is one, not multiple, and gives alternative opinions (for each nation, for each mukallaf, or multiple balances for each believer’s good deeds). It addresses a potential objection from the plural 'balances' in the Qur’an, explaining it is for glorification. Finally, it mentions that Gabriel is the one who weighs, standing with a pillar and tongue, and Michael is entrusted with it. This passage clarifies the eschatological reality of the mizan, distinguishing between the different views held in the tradition, and affirms the unity of the balance while acknowledging other possibilities.

صحائف الأعمال
الموزون

Misconception: The plural 'balances' in the Quran indicates multiple physical balances.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the plural is for glorification; the most correct view is that there is one balance.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the most correct opinion regarding the number of the Balance (mizan)?

The Balance is one, and the majority hold that scrolls of deeds are weighed; the process has a physical analogy and involves angels.

?
The Balance weighs deeds with precision. When you stand before it, what deed do you fear might be too light? And what are you doing today to add weight to it?
?
Gabriel actively looks at the tongue of the scale. Are you aware that every secret action of yours will be placed on that scale? How does that awareness change the way you start your morning tomorrow?
?
The passage mentions that good deeds may be represented as luminous forms. If your deeds were to take shape right now, would they be luminous or dark? What small act of obedience can you commit to today to tip that balance?

The Sirat Bridge and the Seven Arches of Questioning
Sanusi describes the Sirat, its length, and the questioning on the seven qanatir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the Sirat bridge, its length according to different scholars, and the seven stations of questioning that every believer must pass.

Core Idea
The Sirat is a bridge of accountability: each arch tests a core duty.

This lesson takes you into one of the most vivid scenes of the Afterlife — the crossing of the Sirat, the bridge over Hell. Sanusi presents not only its physical description but also the spiritual trials at each of its seven arches. As you read, notice how each religious obligation becomes a checkpoint on the journey to Paradise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the vivid descriptions sink in.
Notice the different opinions on the length of the Sirat.
2
Identify the three opinions on length
The passage gives three different lengths for the Sirat: 3,000 years, 15,000 years, and 7 arches each of 3,000 years. Note who attributes each opinion.
These opinions show the vastness and difficulty of the crossing.
3
Map the seven arches and their questions
List each arch in order and the specific religious duty asked about at that arch. Notice the sequence: faith, prayer, zakat, fasting, Hajj/Umra, purity, and injustices.
The order reflects the importance of each duty in Islam.
4
Reflect on the consequences
What happens if a person completes a duty fully? What if they fall short? The passage mentions detention for a thousand years at each obstacle.
This emphasizes the seriousness of fulfilling obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو على الصراط وقيل : قَبْلَهُ. ثم بعد ذلك يمرُّون على الصراط حتى الكفَّار على الأصح. وقيل: لا يمرون على جميعه، بل على بعضه، ثم يتساقطون في النار وهو جِسْرٌ ممدودٌ على مَثْنِ جهنَّمَ، أوَّلُه في الموقف، وآخرُه على باب الجنة، وطوله مسيرة ثلاثة آلاف سنة: ألف منها صعود، وألف منها هبوط، وألف منها استواء كذا قال مجاهِدُ والضَّحَّاكُ. وقال الفُضَيْلُ بن عِيَاضٍ: بلغنا أن الصراط مسيرة خَمْسَةَ عشرَ ألف سنة : خَمْسَةُ آلاف صعود، وخمسة آلاف هبوط، وخمسة آلاف استواء. وقال سيدي محيي الدين بن العربي: هو سَبْعُ قَنَاطِرَ، مسيرةُ كلّ قنطرة: ثلاثةُ آلاف عام ألف عام ضعُودٌ، وألف عام هبوط، وألف عام استواء، فيُسْأَلُ العبد عن الإيمان على القنطرة الأولى، فإن جاء به تامّاً جاز إلى القنطرة الثانية، فيُسأَلُ كمال الصلاة، فإن جاء بها تامَّةً جاز إلى القنطرة الثالثة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الزكاة، فإن جاء بها تامة جاز إلى القنطرة الرابعة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الصيام فإن جاء به تاماً؛ جاز إلى القنطرة الخامسة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الحجّ وعن العُمْرَةِ، فإن جاء بهما تامين؛ جاز إلى القنطرة السادسة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الطُّهر، فإن جاء به تامّاً جاز إلى القنطرة السابعة، فيُسْأَلُ عن المظالم، فإن كان لم يَظْلِمْ أحداً جاز إلى الجنة، وإن كان قصَّرَ في واحدة من هذه الخصال؛ حُبسَ عند كل عقبة منها ألف سنة حتى يقضي الله بما يشاء. وفي بعض الآثار: أنه يُسْأَلُ في الثانية عن صيام رمضان، وفي الرابعة عن الزكاة.
And it (the Balance) is upon the Sirat; and it is said: before it. Then after that, they pass over the Sirat, even the disbelievers, according to the most correct view. And it is said: they do not pass over all of it, but over some of it, then they fall into the Fire. And it is a bridge stretched over the back of Hell; its beginning is at the standing-place (mawqif) and its end is at the gate of Paradise. Its length is a journey of three thousand years: a thousand of ascent, a thousand of descent, and a thousand of level ground—thus said Mujahid and al-Dahhak. And al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad said: It has reached us that the Sirat is a journey of fifteen thousand years: five thousand ascent, five thousand descent, and five thousand level. And Sayyidi Muhyi al-Din ibn al-'Arabi said: It has seven arches (qanatir), each arch a journey of three thousand years: a thousand years of ascent, a thousand of descent, and a thousand of level. The servant is asked about faith at the first arch: if he brings it complete, he passes to the second arch, where he is asked about the perfection of prayer; if he brings it complete, he passes to the third arch, where he is asked about zakat; if he brings it complete, he passes to the fourth arch, where he is asked about fasting; if he brings it complete, he passes to the fifth arch, where he is asked about Hajj and 'Umra; if he brings them both complete, he passes to the sixth arch, where he is asked about purity (taharah); if he brings it complete, he passes to the seventh arch, where he is asked about injustices (mazalim). If he has not wronged anyone, he passes to Paradise; but if he fell short in any of these qualities, he is detained at each obstacle for a thousand years until Allah decrees what He wills. And in some traditions, it is said that he is asked at the second about fasting Ramadan, and at the fourth about zakat.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage describes the Sirat, the bridge over Hell that all must cross on Judgment Day. It begins by noting a difference of opinion: whether the balance is on the Sirat or before it. Then it states that everyone passes over the Sirat, including disbelievers (the correct view), though some say disbelievers only pass over part before falling. The bridge is described as stretched over Hell, from the standing-place to Paradise. Its length is given as 3000 years (1000 up, 1000 down, 1000 level) according to Mujahid and al-Dahhak, but al-Fudayl says 15,000 years (5000 each). Then Ibn al-'Arabi's view is presented: seven arches, each 3000 years long. At each arch, the servant is questioned about a specific religious duty: faith, prayer, zakat, fasting, Hajj/Umra, purity, and injustices. If he fulfills each completely, he passes; if not, he is detained for 1000 years. A variant tradition mentions questioning about fasting at the second arch and zakat at the fourth. This passage illustrates the detailed eschatological events and the importance of fulfilling all religious obligations.

قناطير (qanatir/arches)
الإيمان (faith)
الصلاة (prayer)
الزكاة (zakat)
الصيام (fasting)
الحج (Hajj)
العمرة ('Umra)
الطهر (purity)
المظالم (injustices)

Misconception: Some think only Muslims cross the Sirat; disbelievers bypass it or fall immediately.
Correction: Sanusi states the correct view: even disbelievers pass over the Sirat (at least partly) before falling into the Fire.

Key Takeaway
The Sirat bridge is a place of ultimate accountability where every major religious duty is examined before entry into Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The seventh arch asks about injustices. Is there someone you have wronged that you have not yet made amends with? What step will you take today to seek forgiveness or make it right?
?

?
This lesson shows that every major duty will be asked about on the Sirat. Are you living with that reality, or do you often act as if accountability is far off? What can you do to keep this truth before your eyes daily?

The Opinions on the Mizan and the Correct View
Al-Bajuri discusses the differing opinions about the number of scales and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the most sound opinion regarding the scales on the Day of Judgment and how to reconcile the Qur'anic plural 'mawāzīn' with the oneness of the Scale.

Core Idea
The Scale is one; the Qur'anic plural is for glorification, not multiplicity.

Al-Bajuri tackles a nuanced point about the afterlife: how many scales are there for weighing deeds? The Qur'an mentions 'scales' in the plural, but sound theology holds that the Scale is singular. In this passage, Al-Bajuri states the correct view, lists other opinions, and then resolves the apparent contradiction with the Qur'anic text. Let's see how he does it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full picture. Notice the different opinions listed.
2
Identify the correct opinion
Find where Al-Bajuri states 'the most sound opinion'. Remember this is the authoritative view in Ash'ari theology.
3
List the alternative opinions
There are three other views mentioned. Write them down to see the range of scholarly discussion.
These are not considered correct, but are recorded.
4
Understand the objection and refutation
The objection comes from the Qur'anic verse using the plural 'mawāzīn'. Al-Bajuri explains that the plural here is for glorification, not number. Also note the roles of Jibril and Mika'il.
This shows how theological reasoning can explain apparent contradictions.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you grasp the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصح أن الميزانَ واحدٌ لا تعدُّدَ فيه. وقيل: لكلّ أمةٍ ميزان وقيل: لكلّ مكلَّف ميزان. وقيل: للمؤمن موازين بعدد خيراته وأنواع حسناته، فلصلاته ميزان، ولصومه ميزانٌ، وهَلُمَّ جرّاً. ولا يَرِدُ على الأول قوله تعالى: وَنَضَعُ الْمَوَزِينَ الْقِسْطَ لأن جَمْعَهُ في ذلك للتعظيم والذي يَزِنُ به جبريلُ، فيأخذ بعموده ويَنْظُرُ إلى لسانه، وميكائيل أمين عليه.
The most sound opinion is that the Scale (al-mīzān) is one, with no multiplicity in it. It was said: For every nation there is a scale. And it was said: For every morally responsible person (mukallaf) there is a scale. And it was said: For the believer there are scales (mawāzīn) equal to the number of his good deeds and the types of his good works, so for his prayer there is a scale, for his fasting there is a scale, and so on. And the first opinion is not invalidated by the verse of the Most High: 'We shall set up the scales of justice' (al-Anbiya: 47), because its plural there is for glorification (taʿẓīm), and the one who weighs with it is Jibril, who takes its pillar and looks at its tongue, and Mika'il is its trustee.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the scales (mīzān) used on the Day of Judgment. Al-Bajuri begins by stating the most sound opinion: the scale is one, not multiple. He then mentions three alternative views: (1) that each nation has its own scale, (2) that each morally responsible individual has a scale, and (3) that the believer has multiple scales corresponding to each type of good deed, such as a scale for prayer, a scale for fasting, etc. He then addresses a potential objection: the Qur'anic verse 'We shall set up the scales (mawāzīn) of justice' uses the plural, which might suggest multiple scales. He refutes this by explaining that the plural is used for glorification (taʿẓīm), i.e., to honor and magnify the scale, not to indicate multiplicity. He further notes that the angel Jibril is the one who operates the scale, taking hold of its pillar and looking at its tongue (i.e., the balance arm), and Mika'il is the trustee (amīn) over it.

Key Terms
ميزان
موازين
تعظيم
جبريل
ميكائيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic phrase 'We shall set up the scales of justice' implies multiple scales.
Correction: The plural 'mawāzīn' is used for glorification (taʿẓīm), indicating majesty, not multiplicity. The scale is actually one.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bajuri, why does the Qur'an use the plural 'mawāzīn' if the scale is one?
A) To indicate multiple scales for different nations
B) For glorification (taʿẓīm)
C) Because each deed has its own scale
D) To show that the scale has multiple parts

Key Takeaway
The most sound opinion is that there is one single Scale on the Day of Judgment; the Qur'anic plural is a form of glorification, and the angels Jibril and Mika'il are entrusted with it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every single act, from the public to the most hidden, will be weighed on one scale held by Jibril and Mika'il, how would your priorities shift today? What is one deed you have been avoiding that now seems too heavy to ignore?
?
The plural 'mawazin' in the Qur'an is for glorification, not for multiple scales—meaning Allah honors the scale by speaking of it in the plural. Does your own speech about the Day of Judgment reflect that same awe? What word or phrase have you used carelessly that need to become more reverent?
?
The scale is one, but your deeds are many. If the only thing that matters is the weight of your good deeds on that single scale, what have you done today to add weight to it—and what have you done that subtracts? Will you stop subtracting before the weighing begins?

Who Crosses the Sirat?
Al-Bajuri presents two opinions on disbelievers crossing the Sirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement over whether disbelievers cross the Sirat bridge.

Core Idea
Even disbelievers cross the Sirat, but only partly, then fall.

After the reckoning and the scales, all people proceed toward the Sirat bridge. A key question is whether disbelievers also cross it or simply fall into Hell immediately. Al-Bajuri presents the most sound opinion and a counterview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the two opinions about disbelievers and the transition to the bridge description.
The passage continues into the bridge description, but focus on the first part.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the phrase 'على الأصح' (most sound) and 'وقيل' (it was said). These introduce the two views on disbelievers crossing.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why scholars differed on this point. The sound opinion holds that disbelievers cross part of the bridge before falling, emphasizing divine justice and the universality of the crossing.
This connects to the wider eschatological narrative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يمرُّون على الصراط حتى الكفَّار على الأصح. وقيل: لا يمرون على جميعه، بل على بعضه، ثم يتساقطون في النار وهو جِسْرٌ ممدودٌ على مَثْنِ جهنَّمَ، أوَّلُه في الموقف، وآخرُه على باب الجنة، وطوله مسيرة ثلاثة آلاف سنة: ألف منها صعود، وألف منها هبوط، وألف منها استواء كذا قال مجاهِدُ والضَّحَّاكُ. وقال الفُضَيْلُ بن عياض: بلغنا أن الصراط مسيرة خَمْسَةَ عشرَ ألف سنة : خمسة آلاف صعود، وخمسة آلاف هبوط، وخمسة آلاف استواء.
Then after that they pass over the Sirāṭ (bridge) – even the disbelievers, according to the most sound opinion. And it was said: They do not pass over all of it, but only over some of it, then they fall into the Fire. And it is a bridge stretched over the middle (mathn?) of Jahannam, its beginning at the standing-place (mawqif), and its end at the gate of Paradise. Its length is a journey of three thousand years: one thousand of ascent, one thousand of descent, and one thousand of level ground – thus said Mujāhid and al-Ḍaḥḥāk. And al-Fuḍayl ibn ʿIyāḍ said: It has reached us that the Sirāṭ is a journey of fifteen thousand years: five thousand ascent, five thousand descent, and five thousand level.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage explains the Sirat bridge, which comes after the scales. Al-Bajuri states that after the reckoning, people pass over the Sirat – including the disbelievers, according to the most sound opinion. However, there is a view that disbelievers do not cross the entire bridge but only part of it before falling into Hell. He then describes the bridge: it is stretched over the middle of Hell, beginning at the place of standing (mawqif, i.e., the gathering place) and ending at the gate of Paradise. Its length is reported as three thousand years' journey (ascent, descent, and level) according to Mujahid and al-Dahhak. A different report from al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad gives its length as fifteen thousand years with the same three segments. This shows that the exact length is not scripturally fixed, and different authorities transmitted different numbers.

Key Terms
الصراط
الكفار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disbelievers do not cross the Sirat at all before entering Hell.
Correction: The most sound opinion is that they do cross it, but only a portion before falling.

Checkpoint
According to the most sound opinion, who passes over the Sirat?
A) Only believers
B) Only disbelievers
C) Both believers and disbelievers
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The most authoritative view holds that disbelievers cross part of the Sirat before falling into Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The weaker opinion says disbelievers fall after crossing only part of the bridge. If you are careless in your duties, how can you be sure you won't fall with them? What specific negligence will you repent from tonight?

Description and Length of the Sirat
Al-Bajuri describes the Sirat's position and length with variant reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical description of the Sirat bridge and the differing reports of its enormous length.

Core Idea
The Sirat is a bridge over Hell with varying reported lengths.

Al-Bajuri next describes the Sirat itself: where it is placed, where it begins and ends, and how long it is. The reports from early scholars differ greatly, indicating that the exact length is not a fixed matter of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the bridge
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the bridge's position: stretched over Hell, beginning at the standing place, ending at Paradise's gate.
The word 'مَثْنِ' may mean 'middle' or 'surface'.
2
Note the three segments of length
The bridge has three parts: ascent, descent, and level ground. Memorize the numbers given by Mujāhid and al-Ḍaḥḥāk.
3
Compare the two reports
Contrast the length of 3000 years with the 15000 years from al-Fuḍayl. Notice the proportional differences.
This shows that the exact length is not fixed in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يمرُّون على الصراط حتى الكفَّار على الأصح. وقيل: لا يمرون على جميعه، بل على بعضه، ثم يتساقطون في النار وهو جِسْرٌ ممدودٌ على مَثْنِ جهنَّمَ، أوَّلُه في الموقف، وآخرُه على باب الجنة، وطوله مسيرة ثلاثة آلاف سنة: ألف منها صعود، وألف منها هبوط، وألف منها استواء كذا قال مجاهِدُ والضَّحَّاكُ. وقال الفُضَيْلُ بن عياض: بلغنا أن الصراط مسيرة خَمْسَةَ عشرَ ألف سنة : خمسة آلاف صعود، وخمسة آلاف هبوط، وخمسة آلاف استواء.
Then after that they pass over the Sirāṭ (bridge) – even the disbelievers, according to the most sound opinion. And it was said: They do not pass over all of it, but only over some of it, then they fall into the Fire. And it is a bridge stretched over the middle (mathn?) of Jahannam, its beginning at the standing-place (mawqif), and its end at the gate of Paradise. Its length is a journey of three thousand years: one thousand of ascent, one thousand of descent, and one thousand of level ground – thus said Mujāhid and al-Ḍaḥḥāk. And al-Fuḍayl ibn ʿIyāḍ said: It has reached us that the Sirāṭ is a journey of fifteen thousand years: five thousand ascent, five thousand descent, and five thousand level.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage explains the Sirat bridge, which comes after the scales. Al-Bajuri states that after the reckoning, people pass over the Sirat – including the disbelievers, according to the most sound opinion. However, there is a view that disbelievers do not cross the entire bridge but only part of it before falling into Hell. He then describes the bridge: it is stretched over the middle of Hell, beginning at the place of standing (mawqif, i.e., the gathering place) and ending at the gate of Paradise. Its length is reported as three thousand years' journey (ascent, descent, and level) according to Mujahid and al-Dahhak. A different report from al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad gives its length as fifteen thousand years with the same three segments. This shows that the exact length is not scripturally fixed, and different authorities transmitted different numbers.
