The Seven Bridges of the Sirat: Questioning on Each
Ibn al-Arabi enumerates seven bridges and their respective questions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Arabi's detailed elaboration of the Sirat as seven bridges, each with a specific question about a religious duty, and the consequences of passing or failing.

Core Idea
On the Sirat, seven bridges test each obligation before Paradise.

Al-Bajuri relates a profound account from the great mystic Muhyi al-Din Ibn al-Arabi, who describes the Sirat as consisting of seven distinct bridges rather than a single span. Each bridge requires a journey of three thousand years and poses a question about a core religious obligation. This passage deepens our understanding of the thoroughness of divine judgment and the importance of fulfilling every duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation once to get an overview of the description.
Notice the structure: the Sirat is divided into seven bridges, each with a specific question.
2
Identify the seven bridges
List the seven bridges in order and the specific question asked at each. Pay attention to the sequence and the condition for passing to the next bridge.
The questions are about faith, prayer, zakat, fasting, hajj/umrah, purity, and wrongful acts.
3
Understand the consequences
Note what happens if the servant brings the obligation complete versus if they fall short. The passage mentions detention for a thousand years at each barrier.
The alternative report changes the order of the second and fourth bridges. Consider why this variation might exist.
4
Reflect on the role of details
Consider how this account illustrates the comprehensiveness of divine reckoning and the need for complete fulfillment of obligations.
This is an elaboration that reinforces the seriousness of religious duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال سيدي محيي الدين بن العربي: هو سَبْعُ قَنَاطِرَ، مسيرةُ كلّ قنطرة: ثلاثةُ آلاف عام ألف عام ضعُودٌ، وألف عام هبوط، وألف عام استواء، فيُسْأَلُ العبد عن الإيمان على القنطرة الأولى، فإن جاء به تامّاً جاز إلى القنطرة الثانية، فيُسْأَلُ كمال الصلاة، فإن جاء بها تامَّةً جاز إلى القنطرة الثالثة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الزكاة، فإن جاء بها تامة جاز إلى القنطرة الرابعة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الصيام فإن جاء به تاماً؛ جاز إلى القنطرة الخامسة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الحجّ وعن العُمْرَةِ، فإن جاء بهما تامين؛ جاز إلى القنطرة السادسة، فيُسْأَلُ عن الطُّهر، فإن جاء به تامّاً جاز إلى القنطرة السابعة، فيُسْأَلُ عن المظالم، فإن كان لم يَظْلِمْ أحداً جاز إلى الجنة، وإن كان قصَّرَ في واحدة من هذه الخصال؛ حُبسَ عند كل عقبة منها ألف سنة حتى يقضي الله بما يشاء. وفي بعض الآثار: أنه يُسْأَلُ في الثانية عن صيام رمضان، وفي الرابعة عن الزكاة.
And Sayyidī Muḥyī al-Dīn ibn al-ʿArabī said: It (the Sirāṭ) is seven bridges (qanāṭir), the journey of each bridge is three thousand years: one thousand years of ascent, one thousand years of descent, and one thousand years of level. So the servant is asked about faith (īmān) upon the first bridge; if he brings it complete, he passes to the second bridge. He is asked about the perfection of prayer (ṣalāh); if he brings it complete, he passes to the third bridge. He is asked about zakāh; if he brings it complete, he passes to the fourth bridge. He is asked about fasting (ṣawm); if he brings it complete, he passes to the fifth bridge. He is asked about ḥajj and ʿumrah; if he brings them complete, he passes to the sixth bridge. He is asked about purity (ṭuhr); if he brings it complete, he passes to the seventh bridge. He is asked about wrongful acts (maẓālim); if he has wronged no one, he passes to Paradise, but if he fell short in any of these traits, he is detained at each of these barriers for a thousand years until Allah decrees what He wills. And in some reports: he is asked on the second about fasting Ramaḍān, and on the fourth about zakāh.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the great mystic Muhyi al-Din Ibn al-Arabi regarding the Sirat. He divides it into seven bridges (qanatir), each requiring a journey of three thousand years (ascent, descent, level). On each bridge, the servant is questioned about a specific religious obligation: first about faith (iman), then about the perfection of prayer (salah), then about zakat, then about fasting (sawm), then about hajj and umrah, then about purity (tuhur), and finally about wrongful acts (mazalim). If he fulfills each completely, he passes to the next. If he falls short, he is detained at that barrier for a thousand years. Al-Bajuri then notes an alternative narration that switches the order of questioning on the second and fourth bridges (fasting and zakat). This account emphasizes the importance of fulfilling all obligations and the detailed examination on the Day of Judgment.

عمرة
طهر
مظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn al-Arabi's seven bridges replace the single Sirat mentioned in hadith.
Correction: His account is an elaboration, not a replacement. It details stages of questioning on the Sirat.

Checkpoint

B) The second bridge
C) The fourth bridge
D) The seventh bridge

Key Takeaway
The Sirat includes seven bridges, each testing a specific obligation, and passing each requires full completion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you are on the first bridge, being asked about your faith. Have you ever truly examined whether your iman is 'complete' as the passage requires? What doubts or shortcomings do you harbor that you have not confronted? Write down one step you will take today to strengthen your certainty.
?
The seventh bridge asks about wronging others. Think of a person you have harmed—even in a small way—whom you have not apologized to or reconciled with. If you were detained for a thousand years for that, would it be worth the pride that keeps you from making amends? Who will you contact today to seek forgiveness?
?
Each obligation is a bridge that can detain you for a thousand years if you fall short. Which of these seven obligations do you feel most deficient in right now? Describe one concrete action you will take this week to fulfill it more completely.

The Questioning by Jibril and Mika'il and the Hooks
Al-Bajuri describes the questioning by Jibril and Mika'il and the angels with hooks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific questioning on the Sirat by Jibril and Mika'il and the symbolism of the hooks as worldly desires.

Core Idea
On the Sirat, angels question you about your life's priorities.

This passage from Al-Bajuri's commentary vividly describes a scene on the Sirat bridge. Two noble angels, Jibril and Mika'il, question every person about how they spent their life, youth, knowledge, and wealth. Meanwhile, other angels stand with hooks that represent worldly desires, ready to snatch those who are attached to them. This lesson unpacks these details and their spiritual significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the scene. Note the key elements: Jibril at the beginning, Mika'il in the middle, the four questions, and the hooks.
2
Identify the four questions
The passage mentions four specific things people are asked about: lifespan (ʿumur), youth (shabāb), knowledge (ʿilm), and wealth (māl). Reflect on why each is significant.
These are major trusts from Allah.
3
Understand the hooks
The angels have hooks (kalālīb) which are described as the desires of this world (shahawāt al-dunyā) taking physical form. Al-Bajuri compares them to thorns of the saʿdān plant to illustrate their painful grip.
This shows how worldly attachments can literally 'hook' a person on the Sirat.
4
Consider the lesson for today
Ask yourself: How am I spending my lifespan, youth, knowledge, and wealth? This passage is a call to accountability and detachment from worldly distractions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجبريل في أوَّله، وميكائيل في وسطه يسألان الناس عن عُمُرِهم فيما أفنَوْهُ، في طاعة الله أو معصيته، وعن شبابهم فيما أبْلَوْهُ، وعن علمهم ماذا عملوا به؟ وعن مالهم من أين اكتسبوه؟ وأين أنفقوه؟ والملائكة واقفون يميناً وشمالاً يختطفونهم بالكلاليب، وهي شهواتُ الدنيا تُصَوَّرُ بصورة الكلاليب مثل شَوْكِ السَّعْدَان كما في الحديث، وهو - بفتح السين المهملة - تَبْتُ ذُو شَوْكِ يَنْبُتُ ببعض الجسور، تقول له العامَّةُ: شَوْكُ عَنتر أصله رَطّب، ثم يبسُ ويتصلب
And Jibrīl is at its beginning, and Mīkāʾīl is in its middle, asking the people about their lifespan (ʿumur) in what they spent it – in obedience to Allah or in disobedience, and about their youth (shabāb) in what they consumed it, and about their knowledge – what did they do with it? And about their wealth – from where did they earn it and where did they spend it? And the angels are standing on the right and on the left, snatching them with hooks (kalālīb), and they are the desires of this world (shahawāt al-dunyā) depicted in the form of hooks like the thorns of the saʿdān plant, as mentioned in the ḥadīth. And it – with the sīn unpointed – is a plant having thorns that grows by some bridges; the common people call it 'the thorn of ʿAntar', originally soft, then it dries and hardens.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage describes the proceedings on the Sirat. Al-Bajuri states that Jibril is at the beginning of the Sirat and Mika'il in its middle. They question people about four things: (1) their lifespan – how they spent it, whether in obedience or disobedience; (2) their youth – how they consumed it; (3) their knowledge – what action they performed based on it; (4) their wealth – from where they acquired it and where they spent it. Meanwhile, other angels stand on both sides, snatching people with hooks (kalalib). These hooks are actually the worldly desires (shahawat al-dunya) manifesting as physical hooks. Al-Bajuri explains that these hooks are like the thorns of the sa'dan plant, a well-known thorny plant, and he notes its common name 'the thorn of Antar' and its physical characteristic of starting soft and then hardening.

Correction: This passage is part of the Sirat crossing; the questioning occurs on the bridge itself.

The Varying Speeds of Crossing the Sirat
Al-Bajuri enumerates the varying speeds of crossing the Sirat based on avoidance of sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the speed of crossing the Sirat varies according to one's avoidance of sins in this world, and the lesson that personal deeds determine one's experience on the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
Your speed on the Sirat mirrors your speed in avoiding sins.

Al-Bajuri presents a vivid picture of the Sirat, the bridge over Hell, where people cross at different speeds. He explains that this variation is not random but directly tied to how quickly a person turned away from forbidden acts in worldly life. The faster one avoided sins, the faster one crosses, and those who were negligent find themselves crawling. This passage reinforces the principle of just recompense and personal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the rhythm. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the parallel structure: 'فمن كان أسرع إعراضاً... كان أسرع مروراً'.
2
Understand the general principle
The first sentence states the rule: speed of crossing corresponds to speed of turning away from forbidden things. Grasp this foundational idea before looking at the examples.
3
Examine the seven categories
The passage then lists seven types of people, from those who cross in the blink of an eye to those who crawl. Identify each category and notice the descending order of speed.
Each category is introduced with 'وبعدهم' (after them).
4
Reflect on the dialogue at the end
The passage ends with a question and answer between a crawling person and Allah. This emphasizes that one's own deeds are the cause of slowness, not Allah's injustice.
This drives home the lesson of personal accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتفاوتون في سرعة مرورهم عليه وبطئه بحسب تفاوتهم في سرعة إعراضهم عن المحارم وبطئه، فمن كان أسرع إعراضاً عن معاصي الله تعالى؛ كان أسرع مروراً، وعكسه بعكسه، ومن توسط في ذلك؛ كان مروره متوسطاً، فالسالمون من الذنوب يمرُّون كطَرْفِ العين وبعدهم الذين يمرون كالبرق الخاطف وبعدهم الذين يمرون كالطير، وبعدهم الذين يمرُّون كالفَرَسِ السابق، وبعدهم الذين يمرون كأَجْوَدِ بقية البهائم، ثم الذين يمرُّون عَدْواً، ثم من يمرُّون حَبْوا، وهم الذين تَطُولُ مسافة الصراط، فيقول الشخص منهم يا رب، لِمَ أَبْطَأتَ بي؟ فيقول: لم أَبْطِئ بك، وإنما أَبْطَاً بك عملك.
And they differ in the speed of their passage upon it and its slowness according to their difference in the speed of their turning away from forbidden things (maḥārim) and its slowness. So whoever was quicker in turning away from the disobediences of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, was quicker in passage; and the opposite is its opposite. And whoever was moderate in that, his passage is moderate. So those who are safe from sins pass like the blink of an eye. After them are those who pass like a flash of lightning. After them are those who pass like a bird. After them are those who pass like a racing horse. After them are those who pass like the best of the remaining animals. Then those who pass at a running pace. Then those who pass crawling, and they are the ones for whom the distance of the Sirāṭ becomes long. So one of them says: O Lord, why have You made me slow? He says: I have not made you slow, but your deed made you slow.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage explains the varying speeds of people crossing the Sirat. Al-Bajuri begins by stating a general principle: the speed of crossing corresponds to the speed with which one turned away from forbidden acts (mahrim) in worldly life. The faster one avoided sins, the faster the crossing. He then provides a detailed classification: (1) those safe from sins cross in the blink of an eye, (2) then those like a flash of lightning, (3) then like a bird, (4) then like a racing horse, (5) then like the best of other animals, (6) then those who run, and (7) finally those who crawl – for these, the bridge becomes long. When the crawling person complains to Allah that He has made him slow, Allah replies that it is not He, but the person's own deed that caused the slowness. This emphasizes personal responsibility and the just recompense.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage explicitly states that speed is proportional to one's avoidance of sins, and the dialogue shows that slowness is due to one's own actions.

Checkpoint

The First to Cross the Sirat: Order of Prophets and Communities
Al-Bajuri enumerates the order of prophets crossing the Sirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the order in which Prophet Muhammad and other prophets cross the Sirat with their communities.

Core Idea
Prophet Muhammad and his ummah cross first, followed by earlier prophets in sequence.

This lesson clarifies the sequence of crossing the Sirat as narrated in some hadiths. Al-Bajuri cites that the first to cross is our Prophet, then Jesus, then Moses, until Noah comes last. The order emphasizes the high station of Prophet Muhammad and his community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the flow. Then read the English translation to confirm understanding.
Notice that the passage names four prophets explicitly but implies a longer sequence.
2
Identify the order of crossing
List the prophets mentioned in order: Muhammad, Jesus, Moses, Noah. Note that each is followed by his community (ummah).
The phrase 'they are called prophet by prophet' suggests a continuous calling until Noah.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why Muhammad and his ummah are first. This highlights his status and the honor of his followers.
This order is based on some narrations, indicating it is a matter of transmitted knowledge (sam'iyyat).
4
Check understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to test recall.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأولُ من يَمُرُّ سيدنا محمد وأمته، ثم عيسى وأمته، ثم موسى وأمته، يُدْعَون نبياً نبياً حتى يكون آخرهم نوح وأُمَّتُه كما في بعض الروايات. وصحْحَ القَرَافِيُّ تبعاً للعِزّ ابن عبد السَّلَامِ أنه عريض، وفيه طريقان: يُمْنَى ويُشرى، فأهل السعادةِ يُسْلَكُ بهم ذات اليمين، وأهل الشقاوة يسلك الشمال. قال بعضهم والأظهرُ أنه مُخْتَلِفٌ في الضّيقِ والسَّعَةِ باختلاف أحوال الناس، كما أن المرور كذلك.
And the first to pass is our master Muḥammad and his community, then ʿĪsā and his community, then Mūsā and his community; they are called prophet by prophet until the last of them is Nūḥ and his community, as in some narrations. And al-Qarāfī, following al-ʿIzz ibn ʿAbd al-Salām, corrected that it (the Sirāṭ) is wide, and in it there are two paths: right (yumnā) and left (yusrā? actually text says 'yushrā' likely meaning left). So the people of happiness are taken along the right side, and the people of wretchedness take the left side. Some said, and the more apparent is that it differs in narrowness and width according to the different conditions of people, just as the passage is also.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage addresses two aspects of the Sirat: the order of crossing and its width. Al-Bajuri states that the first to cross is Prophet Muhammad and his ummah, followed by Jesus and his ummah, then Moses and his ummah, and so on, with prophets being called in order until Noah and his community come last — according to some narrations. Then he mentions that al-Qarafi, following al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam, corrected that the Sirat is wide (not narrow) and has two paths: a right path for the people of happiness (ahl al-sa'ada) and a left path for the people of wretchedness (ahl al-shaqawa). However, Al-Bajuri then notes a different opinion: some say the more apparent view is that the Sirat varies in narrowness and width according to the states of people, just as the speed of crossing varies. Thus, the physical dimensions of the bridge are not fixed but reflect the spiritual state of the individual.

سيدنا محمد
عيسى

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is the first to cross the Sirat?
A) Prophet Noah and his community
B) Prophet Muhammad and his community
C) Prophet Jesus and his community
D) Prophet Moses and his community

Key Takeaway
Prophet Muhammad and his community are the first to cross the Sirat, followed by successive prophets and their communities until Noah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that Prophet Muhammad and his community cross first, do you feel confident that you will be among them? What specific step will you take today to strengthen your connection to his community?
?
The order of crossing reflects divine honor. Are you living your daily life as if you truly desire to be among the first followers of the Prophet, or are you content with a comfortable, mediocre faith? What is one habit you will change this week to align more closely with his teachings?
?
If you were to face the Sirat right now, which prophet's community would you belong to based on your current level of obedience? What is one practical action you can take starting today to ensure you are counted among the community of Muhammad?

The Width of the Sirat: Wide with Two Paths or Varying?
Al-Bajuri discusses two opinions on the width of the Sirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate about the width of the Sirat and the two paths for the saved and the damned.

Core Idea
The Sirat is wide with two distinct paths for believers and disbelievers, though some say it varies by individual.

This lesson examines the nature of the Sirat bridge. Al-Bajuri presents al-Qarafi's view that it is wide and has two paths, for the people of happiness and wretchedness, and also mentions the opinion that its width differs according to spiritual states. Understanding this debate helps us appreciate that the Sirat is not a simple physical bridge but reflects divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation. Note that it contains two distinct opinions.
The first opinion is attributed to al-Qarafi, the second to 'some'.
2
Understand the first opinion
Identify that al-Qarafi, following al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam, corrected that the Sirat is wide and has two paths: right for the people of happiness, left for the people of wretchedness.
The word 'يُشرى' is likely 'يسرى' meaning left.
3
Understand the second opinion
Some said the more apparent view is that the Sirat varies in narrowness and width according to the conditions of people, just as the crossing speed varies.
This opinion emphasizes individual spiritual states.
4
Compare and contrast
Consider how these two views are not necessarily contradictory—they may address different aspects of the same reality.

Source Text

And the first to pass is our master Muḥammad and his community, then ʿĪsā and his community, then Mūsā and his community; they are called prophet by prophet until the last of them is Nūḥ and his community, as in some narrations. Some said, and the more apparent is that it differs in narrowness and width according to the different conditions of people, just as the passage is also.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage addresses two aspects of the Sirat: the order of crossing and its width. Al-Bajuri states that the first to cross is Prophet Muhammad and his ummah, followed by Jesus and his ummah, then Moses and his ummah, and so on, with prophets being called in order until Noah and his community come last — according to some narrations. Thus, the physical dimensions of the bridge are not fixed but reflect the spiritual state of the individual.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sirat is a uniformly narrow bridge for everyone.
Correction: Actually, scholars differed; some said it is wide with two paths, others said its width varies according to one's deeds.

Checkpoint
What did al-Qarafi correct about the Sirat?

B) That it is wide with two paths

The width of the Sirat is a matter of scholarly discussion: it may be wide with two paths, or it may vary according to the individual's state.

?

?

?
When you think of the two paths, does it make you more mindful of the choices you make each day? If not, why?

The Questioning Angels and the Hooks of the Sirat
Al-Bajuri describes Jibril and Mika'il questioning people and the hooks representing worldly desires.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Jibril and Mika'il in questioning people on the Sirat and the symbolism of the hooks representing worldly desires.

Core Idea
On the Sirat, angels question us about our life, youth, knowledge, and wealth.

This lesson opens Al-Bajuri's description of the Sirat by focusing on the questioning angels stationed at its beginning and middle. They ask about four key trusts: lifespan, youth, knowledge, and wealth. Meanwhile, angels stand on either side with hooks that represent the desires of this world, ready to snatch the heedless.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the source passage (up to the description of the sa'dan plant). Let the author's words set the scene before interpreting.
Notice the four distinct questions.
2
Identify the four questions and the hooks
List the four questions asked by Jibril and Mika'il. Then observe how the hooks are described as worldly desires pictured like thorns.
3
Reflect on the eschatological significance
Consider why these specific four areas are questioned. How does this passage connect to accountability in the afterlife?
This aligns with Islamic teachings on reckoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجبريل في أوَّله، وميكائيل في وسطه يسألان الناس عن عُمُرِهم فيما أفنَوْهُ، في طاعة الله أو معصيته، وعن شبابهم فيما أبْلَوْهُ، وعن علمهم ماذا عملوا به؟ وعن مالهم من أين اكتسبوه؟ وأين أنفقوه؟ والملائكة واقفون يميناً وشمالاً يختطفونهم بالكلاليب، وهي شهواتُ الدنيا تُصَوَّرُ بصورة الكلاليب مثل شَوْكِ السَّعْدَان كما في الحديث) ، وهو - بفتح السين المهملة - تَبْتُ ذُو شَوْكِ يَنْبُتُ ببعض الجسور، تقول له العامَّةُ: شَوْكُ عَنتر أصله رَطّب، ثم يبسُ ويتصلب ويتفاوتون في سرعة مرورهم عليه وبطئه بحسب تفاوتهم في سرعة إعراضهم عن المحارم وبطئه، فمن كان أسرع إعراضاً عن معاصي الله تعالى؛ كان أسرع مروراً، وعكسه بعكسه، ومن توسط في ذلك؛ كان مروره متوسطاً، فالسالمون من الذنوب يمرُّون كطَرْفِ العين وبعدهم الذين يمرون كالبرق الخاطف وبعدهم الذين يمرون كالطير، وبعدهم الذين يمرُّون كالفَرَسِ السابق، وبعدهم الذين يمرون كأَجْوَدِ بقية البهائم، ثم الذين يمرُّون عَدْواً، ثم من يمرُّون حَبْوا، وهم الذين تَطُولُ مسافة الصراط، فيقول الشخص منهم يا رب، لِمَ أَبْطَأتَ بي؟ فيقول: لم أَبْطِئ بك، وإنما أَبْطَاً بك عملك. وأولُ من يَمُرُّ سيدنا محمد وأمته، ثم عيسى وأمته، ثم موسى وأمته، يُدْعَون نبياً نبياً حتى يكون آخرهم نوح وأُمَّتُه كما في بعض الروايات. وصحْحَ القَرَافِيُّ تبعاً للعِزّ ابن عبد السَّلَامِ) أنه عريض، وفيه طريقان: يُمْنَى ويُشرى، فأهل السعادةِ يُسْلَكُ بهم ذات اليمين، وأهل الشقاوة يسلك الشمال. قال بعضهم والأظهرُ أنه مُخْتَلِفٌ في الضّيقِ والسَّعَةِ باختلاف أحوال الناس، كما أن المرور كذلك. والراجح أنه أرقُ من الشَّعْرَةِ وأحد من السيف، وقُدْرَةُ الله صالِحَةٌ لمرورهم عليه مع كونه كذلك. والله أعلم.
And Jibril at its beginning, and Mika'il in its middle, asking people about their lifespan in what they spent it: in obedience to Allah or in disobedience, and about their youth in what they wore it out, and about their knowledge: what did they act upon? And about their wealth: from where they earned it and where they spent it? And the angels are standing on the right and left, snatching them with hooks, and they are the desires of the world pictured in the form of hooks like the thorns of al-sa'dan as in the hadith, and it – with the fatha of the important sin – is a plant with thorns that grows on some bridges, the common people call it 'thorns of Antar'; its origin is moist, then it dries and hardens. And they vary in the speed of their passing over it and its slowness according to their variation in the speed of their turning away from forbidden things and its slowness. So whoever was faster in turning away from the disobediences of Allah the Exalted was faster in passing, and its opposite by its opposite, and whoever was moderate in that, his passing was moderate. So those who are safe from sins pass like the blink of an eye; after them, those who pass like the flashing lightning; after them, those who pass like the bird; after them, those who pass like the swift horse; after them, those who pass like the best of the remaining beasts; then those who pass running; then those who pass crawling – and they are those for whom the distance of the bridge becomes long, so one of them says: O Lord, why have You made me slow? He says: I did not make you slow, but your deed made you slow. And the first to pass is our master Muhammad and his community, then Jesus and his community, then Moses and his community; they are called prophet by prophet until the last of them is Noah and his community as in some narrations. And al-Qarafi, following al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam, authenticated that it is wide, and in it there are two paths: the right and the left. So the people of happiness are led along the right, and the people of misery are led along the left. Some said: and the most apparent is that it varies in narrowness and width according to the different states of people, just as the passing is likewise. And the most correct is that it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, and the power of Allah is capable of their passing over it despite it being so. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an eschatological description of the Sirat (bridge) and the varying experiences of people crossing it based on their deeds. It begins by mentioning that Jibril and Mika'il question people about how they spent their lives, youth, knowledge, and wealth, and angels snatch them with hooks representing worldly desires. The passage then explains that people's speed in crossing the Sirat corresponds to their speed in turning away from forbidden acts (mahrim). Those free from sins pass in the blink of an eye; others pass like lightning, a bird, a horse, or other beasts; some run, and some crawl, complaining to Allah that their deeds slowed them down. The first to cross is Prophet Muhammad and his community, followed by Jesus, Moses, and finally Noah, called prophet by prophet. Al-Qarafi and al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam hold that the bridge is wide with two paths: the right for the happy and the left for the miserable. Some say the bridge varies in width and narrowness according to people's states, and it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, yet Allah's power allows crossing.

Key Terms
الصراط
الكلاليب
السَّعْدَان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sirat is only a physical test without accountability.
Correction: This passage shows the Sirat includes a detailed questioning and symbolism of worldly attachments.

Checkpoint
What do the hooks (الكلاليب) on the Sirat represent according to this passage?
A) The sins of the people
B) The desires of the world (شهوات الدنيا)
C) The angels' weapons
D) The thorns of al-sa'dan

Key Takeaway
The Sirat involves questioning by Jibril and Mika'il about four trusts, and angels snatch with hooks that represent worldly desires.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Jibril asks you about your lifespan, what will you say about the hours you spent today? Are they in obedience or have they been wasted? What one change can you make tomorrow to ensure at least one hour is spent in obedience?
?
Your youth has been worn out—how have you used your energy and strength? Is there a specific habit you know you must change but keep delaying? What will you sacrifice this week to reclaim your youth for Allah?
?
The worldly desires are like hooks snatching you from the path. What is the one desire that you know is pulling you away from Allah right now? What concrete step will you take today to cut that hook?

Varying Speeds of Crossing the Sirat
Al-Bajuri enumerates varying speeds of crossing based on deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the speed of crossing the Sirat directly corresponds to one's speed in turning away from forbidden things.

Core Idea
Your speed on the Sirat mirrors your speed in abandoning sins.

After describing the questioning, Al-Bajuri explains that people's speed in crossing the Sirat varies according to their avoidance of forbidden acts. He gives several examples ranging from the blink of an eye to crawling, and even includes a dialogue where a slow person blames Allah only to be told that their own deed delayed them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on varying speeds
Read the Arabic passage from "ويتفاوتون في سرعة مرورهم" to "يبطئ بك عملك". Notice the principle stated first.
Look for the seven categories of speed.
2
Identify the seven categories
List out the seven types of people described: from those who pass like a blink to those who crawl.
They are in descending order of speed.
3
Understand the correlation
Note that each category corresponds to how quickly the person turns away from sins. Reflect on the dialogue at the end.
The complaint and answer emphasize personal responsibility.
4
Apply the lesson
Ask yourself: Which category would I fall into? How can I increase my speed of turning away from sins?

Correction: The passage explicitly links speed to one's own speed in avoiding sins, and the complaint is answered by pointing to one's own deed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines a person's speed on the Sirat?
A) Their age
B) Their wealth
C) Their speed in turning away from sins
D) Their social status

Key Takeaway
The Sirat crossing speed depends entirely on how quickly one abandons sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear the complaint 'O Lord, why have You made me slow?' are you prepared for the answer 'your deed made you slow'? What sin in your life is slowing you down?
?
If the Sirat speed mirrors your sin abandonment speed, what is one sin you are tolerating that you need to abandon urgently?

Order of Crossing and Characteristics of the Sirat
Al-Bajuri discusses the order of crossing and the physical characteristics of the Sirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the order in which prophets and their communities cross the Sirat and the debated features of its width, narrowness, and sharpness.

Core Idea
The Sirat is as fine as a hair and as sharp as a sword, yet Allah's power enables crossing.

Al-Bajuri concludes his description of the Sirat by detailing the order of crossing: Prophet Muhammad and his community first, then Jesus, Moses, and finally Noah. He also discusses scholarly opinions on the Sirat's width: some say it is wide with two paths, others say it varies with each person's state, and the correct view is that it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read from "وأولُ من يَمُرُّ سيدنا محمد" to the end. Pay attention to the order and the opinions about the bridge.
Notice the mention of al-Qarafi and al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam.
2
Identify the order of prophets and communities
List the four prophets mentioned and their communities in order of crossing.
Why is Muhammad first?
3
Analyze the opinions about the Sirat's nature
Identify the three opinions: wide with two paths, variable width, and the correct view of being fine and sharp.
4
Reflect on the meaning
Consider how the physical description (fine, sharp) combined with Allah's power reinforces the idea of the afterlife being beyond normal comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجبريل في أوَّله، وميكائيل في وسطه يسألان الناس عن عُمُرِهم فيما أفنَوْهُ، في طاعة الله أو معصيته، وعن شبابهم فيما أبْلَوْهُ، وعن علمهم ماذا عملوا به؟ وعن مالهم من أين اكتسبوه؟ وأين أنفقوه؟ والملائكة واقفون يميناً وشمالاً يختطفونهم بالكلاليب، وهي شهواتُ الدنيا تُصَوَّرُ بصورة الكلاليب مثل شَوْكِ السَّعْدَان كما في الحديث) ، وهو - بفتح السين المهملة - تَبْتُ ذُو شَوْكِ يَنْبُتُ ببعض الجسور، تقول له العامَّةُ: شَوْكُ عَنتر أصله رَطّب، ثم يبسُ ويتصلب ويتفاوتون في سرعة مرورهم عليه وبطئه بحسب تفاوتهم في سرعة إعراضهم عن المحارم وبطئه، فمن كان أسرع إعراضاً عن معاصي الله تعالى؛ كان أسرع مروراً، وعكسه بعكسه، ومن توسط في ذلك؛ كان مروره متوسطاً، فالسالمون من الذنوب يمرُّون كطَرْفِ العين وبعدهم الذين يمرون كالبرق الخاطف وبعدهم الذين يمرون كالطير، وبعدهم الذين يمرُّون كالفَرَسِ السابق، وبعدهم الذين يمرون كأَجْوَدِ بقية البهائم، ثم الذين يمرُّون عَدْواً، ثم من يمرُّون حَبْوا، وهم الذين تَطُولُ مسافة الصراط، فيقول الشخص منهم يا رب، لِمَ أَبْطَأتَ بي؟ فيقول: لم أَبْطِئ بك، وإنما أَبْطَاً بك عملك. وأولُ من يَمُرُّ سيدنا محمد وأمته، ثم عيسى وأمته، ثم موسى وأمته، يُدْعَون نبياً نبياً حتى يكون آخرهم نوح وأُمَّتُه كما في بعض الروايات. وصحْحَ القَرَافِيُّ تبعاً للعِزّ ابن عبد السَّلَامِ) أنه عريض، وفيه طريقان: يُمْنَى ويُشرى، فأهل السعادةِ يُسْلَكُ بهم ذات اليمين، وأهل الشقاوة يسلك الشمال. قال بعضهم والأظهرُ أنه مُخْتَلِفٌ في الضّيقِ والسَّعَةِ باختلاف أحوال الناس، كما أن المرور كذلك. والراجح أنه أرقُ من الشَّعْرَةِ وأحد من السيف، وقُدْرَةُ الله صالِحَةٌ لمرورهم عليه مع كونه كذلك. والله أعلم.
And Jibril at its beginning, and Mika'il in its middle, asking people about their lifespan in what they spent it: in obedience to Allah or in disobedience, and about their youth in what they wore it out, and about their knowledge: what did they act upon? And about their wealth: from where they earned it and where they spent it? And the angels are standing on the right and left, snatching them with hooks, and they are the desires of the world pictured in the form of hooks like the thorns of al-sa'dan as in the hadith, and it – with the fatha of the important sin – is a plant with thorns that grows on some bridges, the common people call it 'thorns of Antar'; its origin is moist, then it dries and hardens. And they vary in the speed of their passing over it and its slowness according to their variation in the speed of their turning away from forbidden things and its slowness. So whoever was faster in turning away from the disobediences of Allah the Exalted was faster in passing, and its opposite by its opposite, and whoever was moderate in that, his passing was moderate. So those who are safe from sins pass like the blink of an eye; after them, those who pass like the flashing lightning; after them, those who pass like the bird; after them, those who pass like the swift horse; after them, those who pass like the best of the remaining beasts; then those who pass running; then those who pass crawling – and they are those for whom the distance of the bridge becomes long, so one of them says: O Lord, why have You made me slow? He says: I did not make you slow, but your deed made you slow. And the first to pass is our master Muhammad and his community, then Jesus and his community, then Moses and his community; they are called prophet by prophet until the last of them is Noah and his community as in some narrations. And al-Qarafi, following al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam, authenticated that it is wide, and in it there are two paths: the right and the left. So the people of happiness are led along the right, and the people of misery are led along the left. Some said: and the most apparent is that it varies in narrowness and width according to the different states of people, just as the passing is likewise. And the most correct is that it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, and the power of Allah is capable of their passing over it despite it being so. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an eschatological description of the Sirat (bridge) and the varying experiences of people crossing it based on their deeds. It begins by mentioning that Jibril and Mika'il question people about how they spent their lives, youth, knowledge, and wealth, and angels snatch them with hooks representing worldly desires. The passage then explains that people's speed in crossing the Sirat corresponds to their speed in turning away from forbidden acts (mahrim). Those free from sins pass in the blink of an eye; others pass like lightning, a bird, a horse, or other beasts; some run, and some crawl, complaining to Allah that their deeds slowed them down. The first to cross is Prophet Muhammad and his community, followed by Jesus, Moses, and finally Noah, called prophet by prophet. Al-Qarafi and al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam hold that the bridge is wide with two paths: the right for the happy and the left for the miserable. Some say the bridge varies in width and narrowness according to people's states, and it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, yet Allah's power allows crossing.

Key Terms
الصراط
السعادة
الشقاوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sirat is physically impossible to cross.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that Allah's power makes crossing possible despite its extreme fineness and sharpness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is the first to cross the Sirat?
A) Adam
B) Noah
C) Muhammad
D) Jesus

Key Takeaway
The order of crossing begins with Muhammad's community, and the Sirat is finer than hair and sharper than sword yet traversable by Allah's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sirat has a right path for the happy and a left path for the miserable. Which path are you walking on today in your deeds, and what specific action will you change to ensure you are led to the right?
?
The Prophet's community is the first to cross. Do you truly long to be among that community, or have you become complacent in your faith? What one sacrifice will you make today to draw closer to being counted among them?
?
The Sirat is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, yet crossing is possible by Allah's power. Are you relying on your own strength to pass, or are you actively seeking Allah's power through sincere obedience and repentance? What sin are you holding onto that weakens your reliance on Him?

The Sirat Bridge and Divine Power
Al-Bajuri states the preferred view on the Sirat bridge and God's power enabling passage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preponderant view on the Sirat bridge and how God's power enables believers to cross it despite its extreme fineness and sharpness.

Core Idea
The Sirat's extreme fineness and sharpness highlight God's omnipotence.

Al-Bajuri addresses a fundamental question about the afterlife: how can believers cross a bridge finer than a hair and sharper than a sword? This passage presents the preferred theological view, emphasizing that God's power makes the impossible possible. The exact nature of the Sirat is known only to God, but this description underscores both its reality and the divine ability to facilitate passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the key terms: 'الصراط' (the Sirat) and 'قدرة الله' (God's power).
The phrase 'والراجح' indicates this is the preponderant view among scholars.
2
Understand the literal meaning
Compare the Arabic with the literal English translation. Focus on the two descriptions: finer than a hair, sharper than a sword.
These extremes emphasize the difficulty, making God's power more evident.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why does the author mention God's power here? The answer is that human capability is irrelevant; it is God's power that enables crossing.
This affirms God's omnipotence over all things, including the afterlife.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Some might think the Sirat's description makes crossing logically impossible. The passage corrects this by stating God's power is sufficient.
The phrase 'والله أعلم' reminds us that ultimate knowledge belongs to God.

And the preponderant [view] is that it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, and the power of God is capable of their passing over it while it is as such. And God knows best.

The phrase 'والله أعلم' (God knows best) concludes the statement, indicating that the exact nature of the Sirat is ultimately known only to God.

?

Knowledge in the Final Declaration of Faith
Al-Bajuri explains the necessity of knowledge in the final declaration of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the final declaration of faith must be accompanied by knowledge of its meaning to be valid and beneficial.

Core Idea
Mere utterance of the shahada at death is not enough; knowledge of its meaning is essential.

This passage from Al-Bajuri's commentary on Umm al-Barahin addresses a critical nuance about the final declaration of faith. While the Prophet ﷺ promised Paradise for those whose last words are 'la ilaha illa Allah', Al-Bajuri clarifies that this utterance must be accompanied by knowledge of what it entails. Without understanding, the mere words are insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice how Al-Bajuri structures his explanation around two hadiths and a concluding point.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two hadiths
Look for the two Prophetic hadiths quoted. What do they promise? How do they relate to the phrase 'at death uttering'?
The first promises Paradise, the second protection from Hellfire.
3
Understand Al-Bajuri's key point
Focus on the word 'knowing it' (عَالِمِينَ بِهَا). Why does Al-Bajuri emphasize knowledge? What does he say about mere utterance?
This is the core of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: عِنْدَ الْمَوْتِ نَاطِقِينَ... إلخ) أي: لأجل أن تكون آخر كلامهم من الدنيا، فقد روي: «مَنْ كَانَ آخِرُ كَلَامِهِ مِنَ الدُّنْيَا لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ دَخَلَ الْجَنَّةَ» أي: مع السابقين، وروي أيضاً: مَنْ كَانَ آخِرُ كَلَامِهِ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ حَرَّمَهُ اللهُ عَلَى النَّارِ». (قوله: عَالِمِينَ بِهَا أي: بمدلولها، وهو ما دَلَّتْ عليه من العقائد المتعلّقة بالله وبرسله، وإنما أتى بذلك للإشارة إلى أن مجرَّدَ النُّطق بها لا ينفعُ.
(His words: 'At death uttering... etc.') i.e., so that their last speech from the world be it, for it is reported: 'Whoever's last speech from the world is "There is no god but Allah" enters Paradise' i.e., with the foremost. And it is also reported: 'Whoever's last speech is "There is no god but Allah", Allah makes him forbidden to the Fire.' (His words: 'Knowing it') i.e., knowing its meaning, which is what it indicates of beliefs concerning Allah and His messengers, and He only brought that to indicate that mere utterance of it does not benefit.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'inda al-mawti natiqin 'alimina biha' (at death uttering knowing it). Al-Bajuri explains that 'at death uttering' means that the shahada should be the last words of the dying person from this world, citing two hadiths: one promising paradise (with the foremost), and another promising protection from hellfire. Then he explains 'knowing it' (alimina biha) as knowing its meaning, which is the beliefs about Allah and His messengers. He notes that the phrase emphasizes that mere verbal utterance without understanding is of no benefit. Thus, the passage stresses the necessity of knowledge (ma'rifa) along with the declaration of faith at the moment of death.

shahada
akhir al-kalam
natiq
'alim
ma'rifa
nifaq

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that simply saying 'la ilaha illa Allah' at the moment of death guarantees salvation, regardless of understanding.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such utterance must be accompanied by knowledge of its meaning; mere words without understanding do not benefit.

Checkpoint

A) Uttering it loudly.
B) Repeating it many times.
C) Knowing its meaning.
D) Having a religious scholar present.

Key Takeaway
The shahada at death is only beneficial if uttered with knowledge of its meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly know the meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah' as you utter it daily? If you were to die tonight, would your last utterance be accompanied by genuine understanding, or would it be mere habit? What are you doing to ensure that your faith is not just on your lips but in your heart?
?
Reflect on the condition of knowledge. Is your current understanding of tawhid deep enough that you can confidently say you 'know' what you are declaring? If not, what concrete steps will you take today to learn the true meaning of the shahada?
?
The hadith promises Paradise for those whose last words are the shahada with knowledge. But if you were to die right now, would you be counted among the 'foremost' (as-sabiqun)? What does your answer reveal about your readiness for the afterlife?

The Virtue of a Specific Salawat Formula
Relates a dream about Imam al-Shafi'i and the virtue of a precisely worded salawat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extraordinary merit of a specific salawat formula as revealed through dream visions and the scholarly view that its reward is a single greater reward.

Core Idea
A specific salawat formula brings a reward greater than countless ordinary prayers.

The passage introduces a scholarly debate about the reward for a precisely worded salawat and then relates two dream visions that attest to its immense virtue. Through these accounts, we see how a specific formula crafted by Imam al-Shafi'i in his book al-Risala earned him a high station in Paradise and the promise of being spared the Reckoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disagreement about reward
Read the opening lines that present a difference of opinion: does the specific salawat yield multiple rewards (one per count) or one greater reward? Note that the 'verifiers' favor the second view.
2
Read the first dream about al-Shafi'i
Muhammad ibn Abd al-Hakam sees Imam al-Shafi'i in Paradise. Notice that al-Shafi'i attributes his high rank to the salawat in his book al-Risala and recites the exact formula.
The formula is: 'O Allah, bless our master Muhammad as many times as those who remember You remember You, and as those who are heedless of Your remembrance are heedless.'
3
Read the second dream about the Prophet's promise
A righteous person sees the Prophet who declares that al-Shafi'i's reward for that formula is that he will not be stopped for the Reckoning. Reflect on the immense merit implied.
4
Reflect on the significance of the specific formula
Consider why a carefully worded salawat might have such extraordinary virtue. How does this relate to the earlier scholarly disagreement?
This connects the two parts of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف فيمن صَلَّى بنحو هذه الصيغة هل يَحْصُلُ له ثواب بعدد تلك العدة أو يحصل له ثواب واحد، لكنه أَعْظَمُ من ثواب الصلاة المجرَّدَةِ عن ذلك؟ فذهب بعضهم إلى الأول، وذهب المحققون إلى الثاني. وقد حكي أن محمد ابن عبد الحكم قال: رأيتُ إمامنا الشافعي الا الله في المنام، فقلت: ما فَعَلَ الله بك يا إمام؟ قال: رَحِمَني وغَفَرَ لي، وزُفِفْتُ إلى الجنة كما تُزَفُ العروش. فقلت: بماذا بَلَغْتَ هذا الحال؟ قال بما في كتاب "الرسالة" من الصلاة على رسول الله . قلت: وكيف تلك الصلاة؟ قال: اللهم صل على سيدنا محمد عدد ما ذكرك الذاكرون، وغفل عن ذكره الغافلون، فلما أصبحتُ أخذتُ "الرسالة"، ونظرتُ، فوجدتُ الأمر كما رأيت. وقال بعض الصالحين رأيت النبي له الا الله في المنام، فقلت: يا رسول الله، ما جزاء الشافعي عندك حيث قال في كتاب "الرسالة": وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد كلما ذكرك الذاكرون وغفل عن ذكره الغافلون؟ فقال : جزاؤه عندي أنه لا يوقف للحساب.
And they differed concerning one who prays with such a formula: does he obtain reward equal to that count, or does he obtain a single reward but greater than the reward of mere prayer without that? Some held the first view, and the verifiers held the second. It is related that Muhammad ibn Abd al-Hakam said: I saw our Imam al-Shafi'i in a dream and said: What has Allah done with you, O Imam? He said: He had mercy on me and forgave me, and I was escorted to Paradise as a bride is escorted. I said: By what did you attain this state? He said: By what is in the book 'al-Risala' of prayer upon the Messenger of Allah. I said: How is that prayer? He said: 'O Allah, bless our master Muhammad as many times as those who remember You remember You, and as many times as those who are heedless of Your remembrance are heedless.' When I woke up, I took al-Risala and looked and found it as I had seen. And some righteous person said: I saw the Prophet in a dream and said: O Messenger of Allah, what is the reward of al-Shafi'i with you for what he said in al-Risala: 'And may Allah bless our master Muhammad whenever the rememberers remember You and the heedless are heedless of Your remembrance'? He said: His reward with me is that he will not be stopped for the Reckoning.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting a scholarly disagreement about the reward for reciting a specific lengthy formula of salawat: does one receive individual rewards for each unit counted, or a single greater reward? The 'verifiers' prefer the latter. Then it relates two dream visions. In the first, Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Hakam sees Imam al-Shafi'i in Paradise and learns that his high station is due to the salawat formula he included in his book al-Risala: 'O Allah, bless our master Muhammad as many times as those who remember You remember You, and as those who are heedless are heedless.' In the second, another righteous person sees the Prophet who declares that al-Shafi'i's reward for that formula is that he will not be held for judgment. The passage thus emphasizes the extraordinary merit of this particular prayer.

صلاة على النبي
رؤيا
رسالة
الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of salawat are equal in reward.
Correction: This passage shows that a specific, well-crafted formula (like that in al-Risala) brings an extraordinary reward, as confirmed by dream visions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Inclusiveness of the Supplication for the Followers
Commentary on the inclusiveness of the supplication and the temporal phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author broadens the scope of the supplication to include all believers across generations through careful interpretation of key terms and phrases.

Core Idea
The supplication extends to every believer, not just a specific generation, through the meaning of 'followers' and the reinterpretation of 'until the Day of Judgment'.

This passage unpacks two key phrases: 'those who follow them in righteousness' and 'until the Day of Judgment'. The author ensures the supplication covers all believers, including sinners and those who die before the final day. By clarifying the terms, the author resolves a potential objection about the supplication's scope.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scope of 'followers'
The author explains that 'followers' includes even sinners, and 'ihsan' simply means righteous action or faith. Notice how the author expands the meaning beyond the usual understanding.
The hadith of ihsan is mentioned but not applied here.
2
Interpret 'until the Day of Judgment'
The author introduces a deleted noun ('nearness') to reconcile the fact that believers die before the Hour. Understand why this interpretation is necessary.
The belief that believers die before the blast is based on hadith.
3
See how the objection is repelled
The objection that only those alive at the end are included is refuted by interpreting 'followers' as successive groups. Recognize the logical flow.
This shows careful theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَالتَّابِعِينَ لَهُمْ بإِحْسَانٍ أي ولو بمجرَّد الإيمان، فيدخل العُصاة؛ لأنهم أحْوَجُ إلى الدعاء من غيرهم فليس المراد بالإحسان: حقيقته، وهي أن تَعْبُدَ الله كَأَنَّكَ تَرَاهُ»(۱) كما في الحديث، بل العمل الصالح ولو مجرَّدَ الإيمان كما علمت. (قوله: إِلَى يَوْمِ الدِّين أي يوم الجزاء الذي هو يوم القيامة، ولا بد من تقدير مضاف، أي: إلى قُرْبِ يوم الدين؛ لأن الساعة لا تقوم إلا على لكَع ابن لكع)، أي: كافر بن كافر؛ إذ المؤمنون يموتون بريح لَيْنَةٍ تَهُبُّ عليهم قبل النفخة الأولى، فلا يموتُ بتلك النفخة إلا الكفار (۳). ولا يخفى أن المراد بالتابعين: طائفة بعد طائفة، فالمستمر هو الطوائف المتتابعة، لا طائفةٌ ،بخصوصها، فاندفع الاعتراض: بأن الدعاءَ لا يَشْمَلُ إلا من استمرَّ إلى ذلك دون من مات قبله.
His words: 'and those who follow them in righteousness' (wa al-tabi‘ina lahum bi-ihsan) means, even if only by mere faith, so the sinful are included because they are more in need of supplication than others. So what is meant by 'ihsan' is not its reality—which is that you worship Allah as though you see Him—as in the hadith, but righteous action, even if only mere faith as you have learned. (His words: 'until the Day of Judgment' (ila yawm al-din) means the Day of Recompense which is the Day of Resurrection, and it is necessary to estimate a deleted noun, i.e., until the nearness of the Day of Judgment, because the Hour will not come except upon the wicked son of the wicked (laka‘ ibn laka‘), i.e., a disbeliever son of a disbeliever; since the believers die by a gentle wind that blows upon them before the first blast, and none die by that blast except the disbelievers. And it is not hidden that what is meant by 'the followers' (al-tabi‘in) is group after group; so what is continuous is the successive groups, not any particular group. Thus the objection is repelled—that the supplication only includes those who continue until that time, not those who died before it.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the phrase 'and those who follow them in righteousness until the Day of Judgment' from the original text. The author makes several points. First, 'followers' (tabi‘un) here includes even sinners because they need supplication more, and 'righteousness' (ihsan) does not require the highest level of worship (as in the hadith of ihsan) but simply righteous action, even if just faith. Second, the phrase 'until the Day of Judgment' actually means 'until the nearness of the Day of Judgment' because the Hour will not come upon believers—they will die before it by a gentle wind. Only disbelievers will be present at the final blast. Third, 'followers' refers to successive groups, not one specific group, so the supplication covers all believers until the end, including those who die earlier since the sequence continues. This refutes an objection that the supplication only benefits those alive at the end.

إحسان
إلى يوم الدين
تقدير مضاف
الساعة
نفخة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Followers' refers only to the first generation.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'followers' means successive groups (group after group), so the supplication covers all generations.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that the supplication only includes those who continue until the Day of Judgment?
A) By saying the supplication is only for prophets.
B) By saying 'the followers' means group after group, so the supplication continues through all generations.
C) By saying the objection is irrelevant.
D) By saying the Day of Judgment never comes.

Key Takeaway
The supplication for the followers extends to all believers across generations until the end, as clarified through careful interpretation of key phrases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that this supplication includes you, even with your sins, or do you secretly doubt that you are part of 'the followers'? How would your life change if you fully accepted that inclusion today?
?
When you hear the words 'those who follow them in righteousness', do you see yourself as a follower, or have you made excuses that you are too sinful or too distant from the early generations? What step can you take this week to embody that followership?
?
If you knew that the gentle wind could come at any moment and you would die before the final blast, would your current state of faith reflect someone who is a true follower of the righteous? What is the one thing you would change immediately to be ready?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
5 min
Interpreting 'To the Day of Judgment' and 'The Followers'
Explaining the interpretation of 'to the Day of Judgment' and meaning of 'the followers'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'to the Day of Judgment' is interpreted as nearness of that day, and how 'the followers' refers to successive groups, not a specific group.

Core Idea
The Day of Judgment is near, not literal; followers are ongoing groups.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary clarifies two key phrases from Sanusi's creed. First, he explains that 'to the Day of Judgment' cannot be taken literally because believers die before the hour. Second, he shows that 'the followers' in the prayer means successive groups, not a particular group. These interpretations protect the prayer's inclusivity and align with prophetic reports about the end times.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
Notice the opening hadith quote 'as if you see Him' is not fully explained here.
2
Understand the need for an omitted genitive
The author argues that 'to the Day of Judgment' requires assuming an omitted word 'nearness' because the Hour will only come upon the worst disbelievers.
This is a common interpretive technique in kalam to reconcile texts.
3
Grasp the meaning of 'the followers'
The followers are successive groups, not a particular group. This refutes the objection that the prayer only covers those living at the end of time.
This ensures the prayer benefits all believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَأَنَّكَ تَرَاهُ» كما في الحديث، بل العمل الصالح ولو مجرَّدَ الإيمان كما علمت. (قوله: إلَى يَوْمِ الدِّين أي يوم الجزاء الذي هو يوم القيامة، ولا بد من تقدير مضاف، أي: إلى قُرْبِ يوم الدين؛ لأن الساعة لا تقوم إلا على لكَع ابن لكع)، أي: كافر بن كافر؛ إذ المؤمنون يموتون بريح لَيْنَةٍ تَهُبُّ عليهم قبل النفخة الأولى، فلا يموتُ بتلك النفخة إلا الكفار. ولا يخفى أن المراد بالتابعين: طائفة بعد طائفة، فالمستمر هو الطوائف المتتابعة، لا طائفةٌ بخصوصها، فاندفع الاعتراض: بأن الدعاءَ لا يَشْمَلُ إلا من استمرَّ إلى ذلك دون من مات قبله.
'As if you see Him' as in the hadith, rather righteous deed even if mere faith as you learned. (His saying: To the Day of Judgment, meaning the day of recompense which is the Day of Resurrection, and it is necessary to assume an omitted genitive, i.e., to the nearness of the Day of Judgment; because the Hour will only come upon a wicked son of a wicked man), i.e., a disbeliever son of a disbeliever; for the believers will die by a gentle wind that blows upon them before the first trumpet blast, and only the disbelievers will die by that blast. And it is not hidden that what is meant by 'the followers' is group after group, so what continues is the successive groups, not a particular group, so the objection is repelled: that the prayer only includes whoever continues to that [time] and not those who died before it.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'إلى يوم الدين' (to the Day of Judgment) from Sanusi's matn. The author first notes a hadith quotation 'كأنك تراه' (as if you see Him), then proceeds to explain the phrase. He clarifies that 'يوم الدين' means the Day of Recompense, i.e., the Day of Resurrection. However, since the Hour will not actually arrive until the worst of people (described as 'لكع ابن لكع', a son of a bastard, meaning a disbeliever son of a disbeliever) are the only ones left alive, the phrase 'إلى يوم الدين' must be interpreted metaphorically as 'to the nearness of the Day of Judgment', with an omitted genitive ('قرب'). He then explains that believers will die before the first trumpet blast by a gentle wind, so the blast only affects disbelievers. Then he addresses the term 'التابعين' (the followers) in the prayer. The correct understanding is that it refers to successive groups of followers, not a specific group. This repels the objection that the prayer would only include those who survive until the Day of Judgment, excluding earlier believers. The explanation shows careful attention to textual interpretation and theological consistency.

Key Terms
يوم الدين
التابعين
لكع
النفخة الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'to the Day of Judgment' means literally until the Day of Judgment, and that 'the followers' refers to a specific group of companions.
Correction: The author explains that it means nearness of that day, and followers are successive groups, ensuring the prayer covers all believers.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the meaning of 'إلى يوم الدين' in this passage?
A) Until the actual Day of Judgment
B) Until the nearness of the Day of Judgment
C) Until the day of recompense after death
D) Until the end of the world

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'to the Day of Judgment' is interpreted as nearness of that day, and 'the followers' means successive groups, ensuring the prayer covers all believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'and his followers to the Day of Judgment', do you truly intend to include all believers, past and future? This lesson clarifies that 'followers' means successive groups. How can you expand your sense of brotherhood today?
?
The Hour is near for the wicked, but believers die before its blast. Are you living as if you could be taken at any moment by a gentle wind? What one thing will you change this week to prepare for that moment?
?
The omission of 'nearness' could exclude earlier believers. Are there any 'omissions' in your own understanding that lead you to exclude others from your prayers? What assumption do you need to add to ensure your faith is as inclusive as the author intended?
